you know , one of the great things about traveling , and one of the joys of ethnographic research is to live together with the people who can still remember the old days . who still feel their past in the wind , touch them on stones smoothed by the rain , taste them in the bitter leaves of the plants .
simply knowing that jaguar shamans still travel beyond the milky way , or that the meaning of the myths of the oldest of the inuit are still full of significance , or that in himalayas the buddhists still pursue the breath of dharma , is the central revelation of anthropology it is the thought that the world in which we live does not exist in an absolute sense , but only as a model of reality , as a consequence of a group of certain possibilities of adaptation that our own , though successful , call to mind  , many generations ago .
and of course we all share the same adjustment needs .
we are all born . we bring children to the world .
we go through initiation rituals .
we have to deal with the inexorable separation of death , and thus we should not be surprised that we all sing , dance and produce art .
but interesting is the unique sound of the song , the rhythm of the dance in every culture .
it does not matter whether it &apos;s the penan in the borneo forests , or the voodoo acolytes in haiti , or the warriors in the desert of the north of kenya , the curanderos in the andes , or a caravanserai in the middle of the jungle sahara  . this happened to be the colleague with whom i traveled to the desert a month ago . or even a yak-shepherd on the slopes of the qomolangma , everest , the godmother of the world .
all of these people teach us that there are other existential possibilities , different ways of thinking , different ways of orientation on earth .
and that is an idea that , if you think about it , can only fulfill you with hope .
together , the myriad cultures of the world form a network of spiritual and cultural life that envelops the earth and for which earth is just as important as the biosphere network known as the biosphere .
one can imagine this cultural life network as an ethnosphere . the ethnosphere can be defined as the sum total of all thoughts and dreams , myths ideas , inspirations , and intuitions that have been produced by the human imagination since the beginnings of consciousness .
the ethnosphere is the great legacy of humanity .
it is the symbol of all that we are and what we are capable of as astonishingly inquisitive species .
and just as the biosphere was heavily eroded , so did the ethnosphere - only at an even faster rate .
for example , no biologist would dare claim that 50% or more of all species are about to die out because it just is not true . yet , this - the most apocalyptic scenario in the field of biodiversity - hardly corresponds to what we know to be the most optimistic scenario in the field of cultural diversity .
and the deciding indicator is the extinction of the languages .
when each of them was born in this room , 6000 languages ​​were spoken on the earth .
now a language is not just the totality of the vocabulary or a set of grammatical rules .
a language is an expression of the human mind .
it is a means by which the soul of a particular culture finds access to the material world .
every language is like an old forest of the mind , a turning point , a thought , an ecosystem of spiritual possibilities .
and of these 6,000 languages , today , while we &apos;re sitting here in monterey , exactly half of them are no longer whispering in the ears of children .
they are no longer taught toddlers . that is , if nothing is done , they are actually already dead .
what could be lonelier than being shrouded in silence , being one of the last of your people to speak your language , having no opportunity to pass on the wisdom of ancestors , or hoping for children?
and yet , this terrible fate actually happens to someone somewhere on the earth , about every two weeks , because every two weeks , an older person dies and takes the last syllables of an ancient language with him into the grave .
and i know that some of them say , &quot; is not that better?&quot;  would not the world be a better place if we all spoke one language only?  &quot; and i say :  &quot; well , let that language be yoruba . let her be cantonese .
let her be kogi .  &quot;
and then you would suddenly realize what it would be like if you could not speak your own language .
that &apos;s why i &apos;d like to take them on a journey through the ethnosphere , a short journey through the ethnosphere , to try to make them understand what has actually been lost .
now there are some among us who somehow forget that when i say &quot; different possibilities of being &quot; really different ways of being mine .
take , for example , this child of barasana in the northwest of amazon , the people of the anaconda , who believe that mythologically they came up the flow of milk from the east in the belly of sacred serpents .
this is a people that does not cognitively distinguish between the colors blue and green , as the heavenly tent is equal to the forest on which the humans depend .
they have a strange language and marriage prescription called linguistic exogamy: you have to marry someone who speaks another language .
all this is rooted in the mythological past . the peculiarity of these large houses , which speak six or seven languages ​​due to the mixed language , is that you never hear anyone who learns a language .
they just listen and then start talking .
or , one of the most fascinating tribes i have ever lived with , the waorani in northeastern ecuador , an amazing people that was first peacefully contacted in 1958 .
in 1957 , five missionaries tried to make contact and made a grave mistake .
they threw glossy photos of themselves out of the air , which we would rate as a friendly gesture . they forgot that these people from the rainforest had never seen anything two-dimensional in their lives .
they picked up these photographs of the forest floor , tried to see behind the face or the figure , found nothing and concluded that these were business cards of the devil and killed the five missionaries with the spear .
but the waorani not only killed outsiders with the spear .
they pierced each other .
54% of deaths occurred through drilling .
we traced the genealogy back eight generations and found two cases of natural death and when we asked people about it vigorously , they admitted that one of the people had grown so old that he died because of his age and we killed him anyway the spear .
but at the same time they had a clear knowledge of the forest , which was amazing .
their hunters could smell the urine of an animal from 40 paces distance and determine which species of animal it belonged to .
in the early 1980s , i got a really amazing job when my professor at harvard asked me if i was interested in going to haiti and infiltrating the secret societies that were the foundation of the strength of duvalier and tonton macoutes and that poison used to make zombies .
of course , to make sense of this sensation , i had to know something about this remarkable belief of vodoun , and voodoo is not a cult of black magic .
on the contrary , it is a complex metaphysical worldview .
it is interesting  .
if i asked them to enumerate the great world religions , what would they say?
christianity , islam , buddhism , judaism or whatever .
a continent is always left out because the assumption was that there was no religious belief in black africa . of course there was one and voodoo is simply the remnant of these very profound religious thoughts that came over during the tragic diaspora of slavery .
but what makes voodoo so interesting is this living relationship between the living and the dead .
the living give birth to the spirits .
the spirits can be conjured up from under the great water , responding to the rhythm of the dance , to temporarily displace the soul of the living , so that the akolyth becomes god for a brief , shining moment .
deshab , the voodooists like to say , &quot; you white go to church and talk about god .
we dance in the temple and become gods .  &quot;
and because you are possessed , you are taken by the mind . how can you be harmed?
you see these amazing demonstrations: voodoo acolytes in a trance state , touching the glowing coal without burning , an amazing view of the mind &apos;s ability to influence the body to endure the body when put into a state of extreme excitement .
well , of all the people i met , the kogi from the sierra nevada de santa marta in northern colombia were the most extraordinary .
descendants of the ancient tyrannical civilization that populated the caribbean coastal plain of colombia , and as a result of conquest , this people retreated into an isolated volcanic massif rising above the caribbean coastal plain .
in a blood-stained continent , these people were the only ones never conquered by the spaniards .
to this day , they are ruled by a ritual priesthood . however , training for the priesthood is very extraordinary .
at the age of three and four , the young acolytes are separated from their families and locked up in a shadowy world of darkness , in stone huts at the foot of the glacier for 18 years . two nine-year periods , purposely chosen to mimic the nine months of pregnancy they spent in the womb of their birth mother , they now spend metamorphosedly in the womb of their great mother .
and throughout this time , they are culturally introduced to the values ​​of their society . values ​​that maintain the claim that their prayers and only their prayers , the cosmic - or we could say the ecological - balance are maintained .
at the end of this amazing initiation , one day they are suddenly taken outside and for the first time in their life , at the age of 18 , they see a sunrise . and in this crystal clear moment of awareness of the first light , when the sun begins to shine on the slopes of the amazingly beautiful landscape , suddenly everything that you have learned in the abstract is confirmed in astonishing splendor . and the priest steps back and says , &quot; you see , it &apos;s really how i told you .
it is so wonderful . you have to protect it .  &quot;
they call themselves the older brothers and say that we , the younger brothers , are responsible for the destruction of the world .
now this level of intuition becomes very important .
whenever we think of indigenous and landscape , we conjure up either rousseau and the old rumor of the noble savage , which in its simplification is a racist thought or , on the other hand , thoreau and say these people are more connected with nature than we .
thus , indigenous people are neither sentimental nor are they weakened by nostalgia .
there is not much room for it in the malaria-infested marshes of asmat or the cold winds of tibet , but they nevertheless , through time and ritual , have formed a mysterious nimbus of the earth , which is not based on the idea of ​​being consciously close to it , but on a far more subtle intuition , the idea that the earth in itself can exist only because it is inhaled into human consciousness .
well , what does that mean?
it means that a little child from the other who grows up in the belief that the mountain is an apu-spirit who will determine his or her fate will become a totally different person , and another relationship to that resource or place as a little kid from montana who grows up believing that a mountain is a heap of stones that can be worn away .
whether he is a abode of a mind or a heap is irrelevant .
the interesting thing is the metaphor that defines the relationship between the individual and nature .
i grew up in the forests of british columbia and believed that these forests are there to be cleared .
this made me a different person from my kwakiutl friends , who believe that these forests are the abode of hukuk and the bent sky-beak and the cannibal spirits that live on the north end of the world , ghosts that they have at their hamatsa initiation would participate .
now , when you start to consider the idea that these cultures can create different realities , you can understand some of their extraordinary discoveries . for example this plant here .
it is a photo that i took only last april in the northwest of the amazon .
this is ayahuasca , many of whom have heard of it , the most potent psychoactive preparation from the repertoire of the shaman .
what makes ayahuasca so fascinating is not just the pharmacological potential of this preparation , but its processing . it is really made from two different sources . on the one hand , there is this wooden liana , which holds a series of betacarbolines , harmin , harmolin , slightly hallucinogenic . taking this climber alone is like having a blue cloudy smoke on her mind , but it is blended with the leaves of a shrub from the family of coffee plants called psychotria viridis .
this plant contains some very strong tryptamines which are very similar to serotonin , dimethyltryptamine-5 and methoxydimethyltryptamine .
if you have ever seen the yanomami snuffing this stuff , this substance that you make from different species also contains methoxydimethyltryptamine .
snuffing this powder is like being shot at from a gun barrel , surrounded by baroque paintings and landing on a sea of ​​electricity .
it does not produce a distortion of reality , but the dissolution of reality .
i even discussed it with my professor , richard evan shultes , the man who initiated the psychedelic era with the discovery of magic mushrooms in mexico in the 1930s . i claimed that one could not classify these tryptamines as hallucinogenic , because the moment you feel the effects , you are no longer there to experience a hallucination .
but the thing with tryptamines is that they can not be taken orally because they are denatured by a naturally occurring enzyme in the human intestine called monoamine oxidase .
they can only be taken orally in conjunction with some other chemicals that denature the mao .
now the intriguing thing is that the beta-carbolines contained in the liana are mao-inhibitors of exactly the kind necessary to potentiate the tryptamines . so ask yourself .
how can these people find from a flora of 80,000 species of astringent plants these two morphologically unrelated plants that , when combined in this way , create a kind of biochemical version so that the whole is greater than the sum of its parts?
we use this great euphemism , trial and error , which turns out to be meaningless .
but if you ask the indians they say: &quot; the plant speaks to us &quot;
so , what does that mean?
this tribe , the cofan , has 17 varieties of ayahuasca , which they all recognize at great distances in the forest , which appear as a species to our eyes .
and then you ask them how to justify their taxonomy and they say , &quot; i thought you understood planting .
i mean , do not you know anything?  &quot; and i said :  &quot; no .  &quot;
so , it turns out that you take each of the 17 variants at full moon and they sing to one in a different pitch .
well , that does not bring you a doctoral degree in harvard , but it &apos;s much more interesting to count as stamina .
well , the problem - the problem is that even those of us who sympathize with the difficult situation of the indigenous people see them as original and colorful , but somehow confine them to the past while the real world , i . e . our world , progresses .
so , the fact is that you will not remember the twentieth century or 300 years of war or technological innovation , but rather an era in which we were present and the massive destruction of biological and cultural diversity on the planet either actively supported or passively accepted . so the problem is not the change .
all cultures have repeatedly participated in the dance with new opportunities for life .
and the problem is not the technology itself .
the sioux indians did not stop being sioux as they bowed and darted when the americans ceased to be americans when they quit horse and horse wagon .
no change and technology threaten the integrity of the ethnosphere . it is power . the ugly face of the reign .
and wherever you look in the world , you will discover that these cultures are not doomed . these are dynamically living peoples whose existence is superseded by recognizable forces that go beyond their capacity for adaptation . whether this is the monstrous deforestation in the homeland of the penan , a nomadic people from southeast asia , from sarawak , a people who lived free in the forest a generation ago , and has now been degraded to bondage and prostitution on the river bank , from where they see that even the river is polluted with the mud , which almost half borneo seems to sweep away into the south chinese sea . from where the japanese freighters can be seen on the horizon , ready to fill their ship &apos;s rooms with raw tree trunks that they have wrested from the forest . or as in the case of the yanomami , where it is disease carriers that were introduced during the gold rush .
or if we go to the mountains of tibet , where i am doing a lot of research again , you will see the ugly face of political domination .
you know , the genocide , the physical extinction of people is generally condemned , but the ethnocide , the destruction of people &apos;s lives , is not only not condemned , but celebrated in general and in many places as part of a development strategy .
and you can not understand the suffering of tibet if you have not experienced it at the lowest level .
i once traveled with a young colleague 6000 miles from chengdu in western china through southeast tibet to lhasa and only when i came to lhasa i saw the face behind the statistics of which one hears . 6000 sacred monuments , which were turned into rubble and ashes . 1.2 million people killed by the cadres during the cultural revolution .
the father of this young man was identified as a follower of the panchen lama .
that meant he was killed immediately during the chinese invasion .
his uncle fled with her holiness into the diaspora , which brought people to nepal .
his mother was imprisoned as a punishment for being wealthy for crime .
he was smuggled into prison at the age of two to hide under her skirt because she could not live without him .
the sister who did this valiant act was taken to an education center .
one day she stepped on a bracelet maos and for this transgression she had to go to a heavy labor camp for seven years .
the suffering of tibet can be unbearable , but the saving spirit of the people is something unforgettable .
and in the end we are really faced with a choice . do we want to live in a monochromatic world of monotony or in a polychromatic world of diversity?
margaret mead , the great anthropologist said before she died that it is her greatest fear that as we move towards a threadbare , formless , generic worldview , we would not only see the full range of human imagination reduced to a narrowed mindset , but that one day we wake up from a dream , forgetting that there ever was any other possibility .
and it is humiliating to think that our genus may have existed for 600,000 years .
the neolithic revolution that brought agriculture to us and at that moment subjected us to the cult of the seed , replaced the shaman &apos;s poetry with the prosaic prosaic and created hierarchy , specialization and surplus - took place only 10,000 years ago .
the modern industrial world as we know it is barely 300 years old .
well , this superficial story does not tell me that we all have answers to the challenges we face in the coming millennia .
when we ask these countless world cultures for the meaning of humanity , they answer with 10,000 different voices .
and in this song , we will rediscover the possibility of being who we are: people with full awareness , who are aware of the importance of ensuring that all people and gardens can prosper . and there are great moments of optimism .
here &apos;s a picture i took at the northern end of the baffin islands , when i went to narwhal hunting with inuits . and this man , olaya , told me a wonderful story of his grandfather .
the canadian government has not always handled the inuit very well , and during the 1950s , in the wake of the establishment of our rule , they were forced to move to settlements .
the grandfather of this old man refused .
the family , who was afraid for his life , took away all his weapons and tools .
now you must know that the inuit are not afraid of the cold . they used them to their advantage .
the slide rails of their sleds were originally made from fish wrapped in caribou skin .
the man &apos;s grandfather was not afraid of the arctic night or the blowing storm .
he slipped out , lowering his skins from sealskin and defecating into his hand . and when the faeces began to freeze , he shaped them into the shape of a knife .
he sprayed saliva on the sides of the faeces knife and when it was completely frozen he stabbed a dog with it .
he skinned the dog and improvised a harness , took the skeleton of the dog and improvised a sledge , hooked another dog and disappeared over the ice floes with the feces knife on his belt .
so you can not do anything from nothing .
and this is in many ways a symbol of the resistance of the inuit and of the indigenous people of the world .
in april 1999 , the canadian government gave the inuit full control of an area larger than california and texas combined .
it is our new homeland and is called nunavut .
it is an independent area . they control all mineral deposits .
an amazing example of how a nation , a state , can seek and achieve reparation with its inhabitants .
and , in the end , i believe , it is quite obvious , at least to those of us who have traveled to these distant destinations of the earth , to realize that they are not at all remote .
they are someone &apos;s homeland .
they represent parts of the human imagination that go back in time . and for all of us , the dreams of these children , like the dreams of our own children , become part of the geography of hope .
what we at national geographic end up trying to do is believe that politicians never achieve anything .
we believe that polemic - we believe polemic is not convincing , but we believe that storytelling can change the world , and thus we are probably the best institution for storytelling in the world . our website gets 35 million hits every month .
156 nations send our tv channel .
our magazines are read by millions .
and we undertake a series of trips to the ethnosphere , where we take our audience to places of such cultural wonder that they are blinded by the sight . and hopefully , one by one , they will assume the central revelation of anthropology: that this world deserves to exist in different ways , that we can find a way of life in a truly multicultural , pluralistic world , where wisdom is shared by all people can contribute to our mutual well-being .
many thanks  .

i will tell you some things from my book which , i hope , will be in harmony with other things that you have already heard . and i will try to create a few connections myself , if they did not notice them .
i want to start with what i call &quot; official dogma &quot; call .
the official dogma of what?
the official beliefs of all western industrialized societies .
the official dogma works this way: if our goal is to maximize the well-being of our citizens , this is above all achieved by maximizing the freedom of the individual .
the reason for this is that freedom in itself is good , valuable , meaningful , an indispensable prerequisite for the human .
when people have freedom , then each of us acts for ourselves to do the things that maximize our common good and no one has to decide for us .
the way to maximize freedom is to maximize choice .
the more choices people have , the more freedom they have . and the more freedom they have , the more common they will have .
this is , i think , so deeply anchored in our water supply that nobody would come to question it in the same way .
and it is also deeply rooted in our lives .
i will give you some examples of what allows us modern progress .
this is my supermarket . not a big one .
i just want to say something about salad dressing .
there are 175 dressings in my supermarket if you do not count the 10 different extra olive oils and 12 balsamic vinegar that you could buy to make a large number of your own salad dessings , in the unlikely event that none of the 175 in the store will be for you correct is  .
so that &apos;s how the supermarket is .
and then head to the consumer electronics shop to put together a stereo system - speakers , cd players , a cassette player , radio , amplifiers . and in this one electronics business there are so many stereo systems .
we can put together 6.5 million different stereo systems from the components offered in this one store .
you have to admit that this is a big choice .
in other areas - the world of communication .
there was a time when i was a boy , because you could have every phone provider you wanted , as long as he came from ma bell .
they rented their phone . they did not buy it . ™
a consequence of this was that the phone was never broken .
these days are over .
we now have almost an infinite choice of phones , especially in the world of mobile phones .
these are the mobile phones of the future .
my favorite is the one in the middle - mp3 player , nose hair trimmer and creme brulee torch .
and if you have not seen this in your shop yet , you can be sure that you will soon .
and what it does is make people run into the business and ask that question .
do you know now what the answer to this question is?
the answer is &quot; no &quot;
it is not possible to buy a mobile phone that does not do too much .
in other aspects of life that are much more significant than buying objects , the same explosion of choices applies .
healthcare - it is no longer the case in the united states that you go to the doctor and the doctor tells you what to do .
instead you go to the doctor and the doctor tells you: we could do a or we could do b .
a has these advantages and risks .
b has these benefits and risks . what do you want to do ?
and they say , &quot; doctor , what should i do? &quot;
the doctor says a has these benefits and risks and b has those benefits and risks .
what do you want to do ?
and if you say , &quot; if you were me , doctor , what would you do? &quot;
the doctor says , &quot; but i am not her . &quot;
the result is - we call it &quot; patient autonomy &quot;  , that sounds like a good thing , but in reality , a shift is the burden of responsibility for making decisions from someone who knows something - especially the doctor - to someone who knows nothing and is most likely ill and therefore not in the best constitution is a decision to make - especially the patient .
there is tremendous marketing for prescription drugs for people like you and me who , when they think about it , do not make any sense because we do not buy them .
why do you promote us if we can not buy?
the answer is: you expect us to call the doctor the next morning and ask for a change of the prescription .
something as profound as our identity is now a question of choice , as this film is intended to show .
we do not inherit an identity , we invent it .
and we reinvent ourselves as often as we like .
that is , every day when you wake up in the morning , you have to decide what kind of person you want to be .
in terms of marriage and family , there was a time when the standard assumption was that almost everyone had one and they got married as fast as they could and then had children as fast as they could .
the only true choice was &quot; who &quot;  , not when , and not what they did afterwards .
everything is easy to have nowadays .
i teach wonderful intelligent students , and i give them 20 percent less work than i did earlier .
and not because they are less intelligent and not because they are less careful .
it is because they are busy with other things , they ask themselves , &quot; shall i marry or not , shall i marry now?
should i marry later? should i first have children , or only make a career?  &quot;
they are all engaging questions .
and they will answer these questions regardless of whether that means doing all the tasks i assign them and not getting a good grade on my course .
and indeed they should do that . these are important questions to answer .

by the way , there is a corner of which i will not tell anyone where the wirelesslan works .
i will not tell them because i want to use it .
so , what it means , this unbelievable freedom of choice we have to make decisions regarding work , again and again and again , whether we should work or not work .
we can watch our kids play soccer and we have our cellphone on one hip , and our blackberry on the other hip , and our laptop probably on our lap .
and even if they are all turned off , every minute we watch our child mutilating a football match we ask ourselves ,  &quot; should i accept this call?
should i reply to this e-mail? should i write this letter?  &quot;
and even if the answer to the question &quot; no , &quot; it will certainly shape your experience of your child &apos;s soccer game very differently than it would have been .
everywhere we look , big things and small things , material things and lifestyle , life is a matter of choice .
and the world we lived in looked like this .
by that i mean , there were some choices , but not everything was a matter of choice .
and the world we live in looks like this .
and the question is , is that good news or bad news?
the answer is &quot; yes &quot; ,
we all know what &apos;s good about it , so i &apos;ll be sick of it .
all of these choices have two effects , two negative effects on people .
an effect , paradoxically , is that it paralyzes rather than liberates .
with so many options to choose from , people find it very hard to decide at all .
i will give you another drastic example of this , a study on investing in voluntary old-age provision .
a colleague of mine had access to investment files of vanguard , the gigantic fund of about one million employees and about 2000 different jobs .
and what she found was 10 per cent of the funds the employer offered , the participation decreased by 2 per cent .
they offer 50 investment funds - 10 percent fewer employees than if they offered only 5 . why ?
because there are 50 investment funds to choose from , it &apos;s so hard to decide which one to choose , that they postpone it to tomorrow .
and then tomorrow , and then tomorrow , and tomorrow , and tomorrow , and tomorrow , of course , never comes .
do you understand that this does not just mean that people have to eat dog food when they retire because they do not have enough money to travel , it also means that making decisions is so difficult that they miss out on large investment offerings from their employers .
by failing to attend , they spend up to $ 5000 per year on the employer , who would have been happy to increase their contribution .
paralysis is a consequence of too many choices .
and i think it makes the world look that way .
you really want to make the right decision , if it is for all eternity , right?
they do not want to choose the wrong investment fund , or even the wrong salad dressing .
so that &apos;s the one effect . the other effect is that even if we overcome the paralysis and make a decision , we end up less satisfied with the result of the selection than if we had had the opportunity to choose between fewer options .
and there are several reasons for this .
one of them is due to the wide variety of different salad dressings when you buy one and it is not perfect - and which salad dressing is already perfect? it &apos;s easy to imagine that you could have made another decision that would have been better . and what happened this pictorial alternative causes them to regret the decision , and this repent negatively affects the satisfaction they had of their decision made , even if it was a good decision .
the more possibilities there are , the easier it is not to regret anything that is disappointing for the choice you make .
secondly , what economists call oppertunity costs .
dan gilbert has made a big point this morning by telling how much the way in which we rate things depends on what we compare .
well , if there are many alternatives to consider , it is easy to think of attractive features of alternatives that exclude them , making them less satisfied with the alternatives they have chosen .
here is an example  . for those of you who are not new yorkers , i apologize .
but here is what they should have thought .
here are a few on the hamptons .
very expensive real estate .
beautiful beach . great day  . they have it all for themselves .
what could be better ?  &quot; damned  &quot; the man thinks , &quot; it is august .
everyone in my manhattan neighborhood is gone .
i could park right in front of my house .  &quot;
and he spends 2 weeks of the thought that he has missed the opportunity to have a great parking day after day .
oppertunity costs lower the satisfaction we receive through our selection , even if what we have chosen is fantastic .
and the more possibilities there are to choose from , the more attractive features of this selection are taken into account in the oppertunity costs .
here is another example .
well , this cartoon makes some statements .
he makes a statement about it in the now to live and maybe also about things to do slowly .
but one important point he makes is that whenever you decide to do something , you decide not to do other things .
and these other things may have many attractive features , and what they will do will be less appealing .
third , escalation of expectations .
this hit me when i wanted to exchange my jeans .
i wear jeans , almost all the time .
and there was a time when there were only jeans of one type , and they bought them , and they did not fit well , they were incredibly uncomfortable and if they had worn them long enough and washed them enough , they started &quot; ok  &quot; to feel .
so , to buy new jeans after many years of wearing the old ones , and i said , &quot; i &apos;d like some jeans , here &apos;s my size . &quot;
and the seller said , &quot; do you want slim , light-fitting or wide?
do you want buttons or zippers? stone or acid washed?
do you want to puncture jeans?
you want to cut them down wide , conical , blah blah blah  . . . &quot;  , and so on he said .
my jaw was down and after i recovered i said , &quot; i &apos;d like to have the kind that used to be the only type . &quot;
he had no idea what that was , so i spent an hour trying on all those damn jeans and left the shop - and i say the truth - with the best matching jeans i &apos;ve ever had .
i had it better . all of these choices allowed me to do better .
but i felt worse .
why ? i wrote a whole book to try and explain it to myself .
the reason that i felt worse , with all these options available , my expectations of what a good pair of jeans was , went up .
i had very low expectations . i had no concrete expectations , as they existed only in one way .
when they came in 100 species , damn , one of them should have been perfect,
and what i got was good , but it was not perfect .
so i compared what i got to what i expected , and what i had was disappointing compared to what i expected .
adding opportunities to people &apos;s lives will inevitably increase expectations people will have about how good those options will be .
and this leads to less satisfaction with the results , even if the results are good .
nobody in the world of marketing knows this . because if they did , they would all not know what this is about .
the truth is more like this .
the reason is that in the past everything was better when everything was worse , when everything was worse , it was possible for the people to make experiences that were a pleasant surprise .
nowadays , in the world we live in - we are wealthy , industrialized citizens , with perfection as expectation - the best you can ever hope for is that things are as good as you expect them to be .
they are never pleasantly surprised . because your expectations , my expectations , are high .
the secret to happiness - that &apos;s what they all came for - the secret to happiness is low expectations .
i would like to say - a short autobiographical moment - that i am married to a woman and she is really very wonderful .
i could not have done better . i did not settle down .
but settling down is not always such a bad thing .
ultimately , a consequence of buying a bad-fitting jeans when there is only one style to buy is that when they are dissatisfied , they ask themselves why , who is responsible , the answer is clear . the world is responsible . what could they have done?
when hundreds of different types of jeans are available , and they buy one that is disappointing , and they ask why , who is responsible?
it is equally clear that the answer to the question is: you .
they could have done better .
with 100 different types of jeans in the shop window , there is no excuse for mistakes .
and so , when people make decisions , and even if the results of the decisions are good , they feel disappointed , they blame themselves .
clinical depression exploded in the industrial world in the last generation .
i believe a significant - not the only one , but a significant contributor to this explosion of depression , and even suicide , is that people have experienced disappointment because their standards are so high . and then , if you have to explain yourself these experiences , you think it &apos;s your fault .
and so the balance sheet result is that we are generally better , objectively , and feel worse .
so let me remind you .
this is the official dogma , that one that we all consider right is completely wrong . it is not true  .
there is no question that some choice is better than none , but it does not follow that more choice is better than some choice .
there is a magical amount . i do not know what it is  .
i &apos;m pretty sure that we missed this point for a long time in the selection of our well-being improved .
now , as a matter of principle - i &apos;m almost done - as a matter of principle , the thing to think about is . what makes all this choice possible in the industrialized societies is the material wealth .
there are many places in the world , and we have heard of many of them whose problem is not the big choice .
your problem is that you have too little .
so the things i &apos;m talking about are a weird problem of modern , rich , western societies .
and what is so frustrating and annoying is: steve levitt told them yesterday how these expensive and hard to install child seats do not help . it is a waste of money .
what i tell them is that these expensive , complicated decisions - it &apos;s not easy they do not help .
they even hurt .
they make sure that we stand there worse .
if something of what would allow people of our society to make decisions on societies where people have not chosen enough , would not only improve people &apos;s lives , but ours would also be improved .
economists call this a pareto improvement application .
revenue redistribution will make everyone &apos;s better - not just the poor - because all of this big choice is plaguing us .
in conclusion , please read this cartoon , and , a highly developed person , says , &quot; ah , what does this fish know?
you know nothing in this fishbowl .  &quot;
impoverished imagination , a shortsighted view of the world - and that &apos;s the way i read it first .
but the more i thought about it , the more i came to believe that the fish knows something .
for the truth is that if you break the fishbowl so that everything is possible , you have no freedom . you have paralysis .
if you break the fishbowl so that everything is possible , lower the satisfaction .
they increase paralysis and reduce satisfaction .
everyone needs a fishbowl .
this is almost certainly too limited - possibly also for the fish , definitely for us .
but the loss of a metaphorical fishbowl is a recipe for miserable , and , i suppose , disaster .

you know , i &apos;ve talked about some of these projects before , about the human genome and what that might mean , and about discovering new groups of genes .
in fact , we are starting from a new point: we have digitized biology and now we are trying to move from digital code to a new phase of biology by designing and building new life .
we have always tried to ask significant questions .
what is life? it is something that i believe many biologists have tried to understand at different levels .
we have tried different approaches to simplify it to the minimum components .
we digitized nearly 20 years . when we sequenced the human genome , the shift from the analogue world of biology to the digital world of computers took place .
now we are trying to ask if we can regenerate life or if we can create new life from this digital universe?
this is the map of a small organism , mycoplasma geitalium , that has the smallest genome of a species that can replicate itself in the lab . and we tried to do it with an even smaller genome .
we managed to do without about one hundred genes out of the 500 or so that are here .
but if we look at this metabolic map , which is very simple compared to ours . trust me , that &apos;s simple . but if we look at all those genes that we can eliminate one by one , it is very unlikely that this would lead to a living cell .
therefore , we decided that the only way forward is to actually synthesize this chromosome , which allows us to vary the individual components and thus ask some of these fundamental questions .
and so we started with the question , can we synthesize a chromosome?
does chemistry allow you to create these really big molecules that have never been done before?
and , if we can , can we start the chromosome?
by the way , a chromosome is just one piece of inert chemical material .
our steps in digitizing life have been exponential .
our ability to write genetic code has developed very slowly , but it has increased nonetheless . and our last point would now be an exponential curve .
we started 15 years ago .
it actually took several years starting with a bioethical review before we made the first experiments .
as it turned out , dna synthesis is very difficult .
there are tens of thousands of machines around the world that can make small pieces of dna , 30 to 50 characters long , but it &apos;s a degenerative process , so the longer you make a piece , the more mistakes happen .
so we had to develop a new method with which we could assemble these small pieces and correct the mistakes .
that was our first attempt , starting with the digital information , the phi x 174 genome .
it is a small virus that kills bacteria .
we made small pieces , made a mistake correction and had a dna molecule of about 5,000 letters .
the exciting phase came when we took this piece of inert chemical and put it in a bacterium , and the bacterium began reading the genetic code and made viral particles .
the viral particles were then released from the cells and came back , killing e . coli .
recently i spoke to the oil industry and they said that they understood this model exactly .
they laughed more than you do here .
so we think that this is a situation where software can actually build your own hardware in a biological system .
but we wanted to go much further . we wanted to build an entire bacterial chromosome . it has more than 580,000 letters of genetic code . so we thought , we build it in cassettes of the size of the virus with which we could vary the size of the cassettes to understand what the individual components of a living cell are .
design is the key , and if you want to start with digital information in the computer , this digital information must be really error-free .
when we first sequenced the genome in 1995 , the error rate was one error per 10,000 base pairs .
we found 30 errors when resequencing . if we had used the original sequence , it would never have been possible to boot .
part of the design is to create pieces that are 50 characters long and overlap with other 50-letter pieces to make smaller sub-units fit together .
we have incorporated unique elements here .
you may have heard that we have water marks installed .
think about it: we have a 4-letter code: a , c , g and t .
triplets of these letters - these letters encode about 20 amino acids; and here is a single determination for each of these amino acids .
so we can use the genetic code to write our words , sentences , thoughts .
at first we only signed .
some people were disappointed that it was not poetry .
we designed the pieces so that we could simply disassemble them with enzymes . there are enzymes that repair and reassemble them .
and we started making pieces starting with pieces containing five to seven thousand letters , adding them to each other to make 24,000-letter pieces to re-assemble , up to 72,000 .
in each step , we produced these pieces in excess so we could sequence them because we were trying to create a very robust process that you will see in a minute .
we tried to get to the point of automation .
so , that looks like a basketball playoff .
if we get to these really big pieces - with over 100,000 base pairs - then these will not be easy in e . coli grow . it exceeds all possibilities of modern molecular biology . and so we turned to other mechanisms .
we knew about a mechanism called homologous recombination that biology uses to repair dna that can put together such pieces .
here is an example of it . there is an organism called the deinococcus radiodurans that can survive three million rads of radiation .
in the upper field you can see that its chromosome is simply falling apart .
12 to 24 hours later , everything has reassembled as it was .
we have thousands of organisms that can do that .
these organisms can be totally dried out , they can even live in vacuum .
i am absolutely sure that living in space can exist and move in search of aquatic conditions .
indeed , nasa has shown that there is much of it out there .
here is a picture of the molecule we made by using these processes - in fact we only used yeast mechanisms with the right design of the pieces we fit them . hefe then automatically merges these .
this is not an electron micrograph , it is a regular microscope image .
it &apos;s such a big molecule that we can even see it with the light microscope .
these are recordings over a duration of 6 seconds .
this is the publication that we recently completed .
that &apos;s over 580,000 letters of genetic code . it is the largest molecule of defined structure ever created by humans .
it has a molecular weight of over 300 million .
if we were to print it out with a font size of 10 and without spacing , it would cover 142 pages just to print that genetic code .
now , how do we start a chromosome? how do we activate it?
it is obvious that it is very simple with a virus . it is much more complicated to deal with bacteria .
it &apos;s just as easy to do it in eukaryotes as we are: you just take out the nucleus and put in another , and that &apos;s exactly what they all heard about cloning .
in archaebacteria , the chromosome is integrated into the cell but only recently have we shown that one can perform a complete transplantation of a chromosome from one cell to another and activate it .
we purified the chromosome of a microbial species . roughly speaking , these two are as far apart as humans and mice . we added some extra genes so we could select this chromosome . we decompose it with enzymes to destroy all proteins . and it was amazing when we put it in a cell - and you will appreciate our very mature graphics here -
the new chromosome went into the cell .
in fact , we thought this was the end , but we tried to take the process a little further .
that &apos;s the main mechanism of evolution here .
we find all sorts of species here that have taken up a second chromosome or a third from somewhere , adding thousands of characteristics in just one second each to a species .
humans who imagine evolution as one gene being changed at a time have missed much of biology .
there are enzymes called restriction enzymes that actually digest dna .
the chromosome that was in the cell has none; the chromosomes we have already inserted .
it was expressed in the cell and recognized the other chromosome as a foreign material digested it and so we ended up with only one cell with a new chromosome .
it turned blue because of the gene we had inserted .
and after a very short time , it had lost all of the characteristics of the species and completely converted to a new species based on the new software we put into the cell .
all proteins changed membranes - when we read the genetic code , it was exactly what we transferred .
it may sound like genomic alchemy , but we can change things quite dramatically by moving the software dna .
now i have argued that this is not the genesis - this is based on 3.5 billion years of evolution
and i have argued that we may be creating a new form of the cambrian explosion in which there could be massive new species formation based on this digital design .
why should we do that?
i think it &apos;s pretty obvious in terms of what we need .
we are about to grow from six and a half to nine billion people in the next 40 years .
to put it in my context: i was born in 1946 .
today there are three people on this planet for anyone who existed in 1946; it will be four within 40 years .

it will be a challenge to offer this for 9 .

when we think about biological processes or any process to replace them , it becomes a huge challenge .
then , of course , there is all the co2 of this material that ends up in the atmosphere .

the electronics industry had dozens of components and look at the variety that resulted .

at the last ted conference i gave an introduction to the lhc .
and i promised to come back to explain how the machine works .
so now it is time  . and for all those who were not there at the time , the lhc is the largest scientific experiment ever undertaken - 27 kilometers in diameter .
his job is to create the conditions that existed less than a billionth of a second after the beginning of the universe - up to 600 million times in a second .
it &apos;s just breathtakingly ambitious .
this is the machine below geneva .
we take pictures of these mini-big bangs in detectors .
i work on this . he calls himself atlas detector - 44 meters in width , 22 meters in diameter .
here a spectacular image of the atlas in the construction so you see the proportions .
on the 10th of september last year the machine started for the first time .
and this picture was taken by atlas .
it caused immense cheers in the control room .
it is a picture of the first particle of radiation that covered the entire distance around the lhc , then deliberately collided with a part of the lhc to make a rain of particles patter on the detector .
in other words , when we saw this picture on the 10th of september , we knew the machine was working , which is a big triumph .
i do not know if this caused the biggest cheers , or this when someone visited the google page and found the homepage like this .
it means we achieved a cultural influence alongside the scientific .
about a week later there was a problem with the machine which had to do with these wires here - these golden wires here
these wires conduct 13-thousand ampere when the machine is running at high power .
well , the engineers among you will look at them and say , &quot; no , they will not do that , these are little wires . &quot;
they can do that because if you cool them very far , they become superconductors .
at minus 271 degrees , colder than the space between the stars , these wires can withstand the tension .
in one of the connections , between over nine thousand magnets in the lhc , there was a manufactory defect .
as a result wires are slightly heated and 13-thousand amps suddenly encountered electrical resistance .
this was the result .
well , all of this is even more impressive if you imagine that the magnets weigh over 20 tons and move by about 30 cm .
so we damaged about 50 of the magnets
and had to remove them , which we did .
we conditioned them , repaired them .
they are all on their way back now .
by the end of march the lhc will be operational again .
we will turn it on , and expect to gather first data in june or july , and continue our journey to find out what the building blocks of the universe are .
well , of course , in a way such accidents rekindled the debate about the value of science and engineering at such borders . it is easy to reject such a thing .
i think the fact that it is so hard , the fact that we reach beyond our borders , is the value of things like the lhc .

i am a writer .
writing books is my job , but of course it &apos;s more than that .
it is also my big , lifelong love and fascination .
and i do not expect that will ever change .
however , there has recently been something weird in my life and career that has led me to completely rethink my relationship to this work .
this strange event was that i recently wrote a book - a biography called &quot; eat , pray , love&quot;  - ​​which , unlike any of my previous books , went out to the world for some reason and a great mega-event , international bestseller -ding was .
the result is that no matter where i go , i &apos;m treated as if i was doomed .
seriously - doomed!
for example , they approach me with great concern and say , &quot; are not you afraid that you will never exceed this success?
are you not afraid that you will continue writing your whole life and never again create a book that interests anyone in the world? never again ? &quot; 
okay , that really makes you brave .
however , it would be worse if i did not remember that over 20 years ago , when i first told teenagers that i wanted to be a writer , i was confronted with the same kind of fearful reaction .
people said , &quot; are not you scared never to succeed?
are not you afraid that the humiliation of a cancellation kills you?
are not you scared that you &apos;re going to do this job all your life and that nothing will ever happen to it and that you &apos;ll die on a pile of broken dreams - your mouth filled with the bitter ashes of failure? &quot; 
that &apos;s about it , you know .
the answer - the short answer to all these questions is: &quot; yes . &quot; 
yes , i am afraid of all these things .
and i always had .
and i &apos;m afraid of many more things that nobody would guess .
like algae - and other scary things .
but when it comes to writing i think about it recently and ask myself: why?
is that sensible?
is it logical that someone should be expected to be afraid to do the job he feels called to do?
and what exactly is wrong with the creative activities that make us uncertain about the mutual state of mind - in a way that other professions do not?
my father , for example , was a chemist and i can not remember a situation in his 40 years as a chemist , when he was asked if he was afraid to be a chemist .
with your chemist blockade , john - how are you? s you with it?
it just did not happen , you know?
but to be fair - the group of chemists has not received the reputation of alcoholic manic-depressive people over the centuries .
but we writers seem to have that kind of reputation . but not only the writers , but the creators of all genres , seem to have this reputation of being mentally unstable mentally .
all you have to do is look at the bitter death toll of truly great people from the twentieth century alone who died young and often by their own hands .
and even those who did not actually commit suicide seemed genuinely neglected by their talents .
norman mailer said shortly before his death in his last interview , &quot; each of my books has killed me a bit further . &quot; 
an extraordinary statement that you can make about your life &apos;s work .
but we do not even flinch when we hear someone say something like that , because we know this kind of statement for so long . somehow we have internalized and accepted the concept in such a way that creativity and sorrow somehow naturally belong together and that art - in the end - always leads to the soul &apos;s distress .
the question that i would like to ask everyone here today is: is this idea completely alright for you?
do you feel comfortable with it? because with only one centimeter apart  . . . i feel with this notion at all well .
i think it &apos;s disgusting .
and i find them dangerous . and i do not want to see it taken over in the next century .
i find it better when we encourage our great creative to live .
and i definitely know that for me - in my situation - it would be very dangerous to begin to soak down this dark path of guessing - especially in the circumstance that i am in my career right now .
that is - look: i &apos;m pretty young . i am only about 40 years old .
i may have another 40 years of work in my life .
and it is very likely that everything i write from now on will be judged by the world as the work that came after the crazy success of my last book , right?
i &apos;ll say it straight out , because we &apos;re all kind of friends here - it &apos;s very likely that my biggest success is behind me .
oh , jesus , what a thought!
these are the kind of thoughts that can make someone start gin drinking at 9 o &apos;clock in the morning . and i do not want that  .
i would rather continue to do this work that i love .
so the question is: how?
after some time of reflection , it seems to me that in order to continue writing , i have to design some kind of protective psychological construct .
somehow , from now on , i have to find a way to keep writing safe distance between myself and my very natural oppression about how the reaction will be to the written .
while i was looking for models to do that over the past year , i also searched the past and tried to find other companies to see if they had any better or more reasonable ideas than we did to help creative people deal with the emotional risks associated with creativity .
this search took me to ancient greece and ancient rome .
please follow me , because the circle will close again .
but in ancient greece and rome , people did not believe that creativity comes from people , okay?
they believed that creativity was a divine servant spirit coming to people from a distant , unknown source - for reasons unknown , unknown .
the greeks called these divine servants spirits &quot; demon&quot;  of creativity .
socrates is known to believe that he had a demon who told him wisdom from afar .
the romans had the same idea , calling this kind of disembodied creative mind a &quot; genius&quot;  .
that &apos;s great  . the romans did not really think that a genius was a particularly smart individual .
they believed that genius was that kind of magical divine being that was believed to live literally in the walls of an art studio - much like dobby , the house elf - who came out and assisted the artist in an invisible way and the result work .
that &apos;s brilliant - there it is , the distance i spoke of - the psychological construct that protects you from the consequences of your work .
and everyone knew it worked that way , right?
the artist of antiquity was protected from certain things , such as , for example , too much narcissism , right?
if your work was brilliant , you could not view everything for yourself . everyone knew that you had that bodiless genius that helped you .
if your work flopped - not entirely your mistake , right?
everyone knew your genius was a bit lame .
so the people in the west have really thought about creativity for a long time .
and then came the renaissance and everything changed . we had this great idea . and this great idea was: let us place the human individual in the center of the universe - over all gods and mysteries . and there is no room left for mystical creatures to accept orders from the divine .
it is the beginning of rational humanism and people began to believe that creativity came completely from their own individual .
for the first time in history , people hear people call this genius a genius - instead of having a genius .
i have to tell them: i think that was a big mistake .
you know ? i think allowing a mere person to think that he or she is like the vessel , like the pool and the essence and the source of all divine , creative , unknown , infinite mysteries is a little bit too much responsibility to make them one to impose fragile , human psyche .
it &apos;s like asking someone to swallow the sun .
that disfigures and distorts whole egos and it generates all these unattainable expectations of success .
i think that this pressure of success has destroyed our artists of the past 500 years .
and , if that &apos;s true - and i think it &apos;s true - the question is:
can we change it?
perhaps to return to an ancient understanding of the relationship between people and the mystery of the creative .
maybe not  .
maybe we can not just erase the mindset of 500 years of rational humanism in an 18-minute lecture .
and there are certainly people in this audience who would really have legitimate scientific doubts about the notion of - basically - fairies that follow people and apply fairy magic to their projects , etc .
i probably will not be able to take all of them on this trip .
but the question i want to raise is: why not?
why not think about it?
because it makes as much sense as anything else that i &apos;ve heard of when it comes to explaining the absolutely maddening caprice of the creative process .
a process that - as anyone who has ever tried to do something - in other words: everyone here - not always behaving reasonably .
in fact , sometimes he can feel downright paranormal .
i met recently when i met the extraordinary american poet ruth stone , now over 90 years old , but a poet all her life . she told me that when she was growing up in village virginia she was working out in the fields . she said she could feel and hear a poem about the landscape approaching her .
she said it was like a thundering train of air,
that would come to them over the land .
she felt when it came , because it would make the earth shake under her feet .
she knew she could only do one thing at such a moment . and that was - in her words - &quot; run like a devil&quot;  .
she ran like a devil to the house while being persecuted by this poem . all that mattered was that she had to get to a sheet of paper and a pen quickly enough so that when it thundered through it , she could catch it and hold onto the sheet .
other times she was not fast enough . she ran and ran and ran , but she did not reach the house and the poem rolled through her and she missed it . she said it would continue to cross land and , as she said , &quot; look for another poet&quot;  .
and then there were those moments - the part i &apos;ll never forget - she said there were moments when she almost missed it .
so: she runs to the house and searches for the paper and the poem goes through her and she grabs a pen just as it passes through her and then - she said - it was like grabbing it with her other hand and grabbing it to get .
she caught the poem on his tail and pulled it back into her body and took it on the sheet of paper .
on these occasions , the poem arrived impeccably and intact on the paper - but backwards - from the last word to the first .
when i heard that , i thought , &quot; that &apos;s scary - that &apos;s exactly how my creative process works . &quot; 
that &apos;s not like my creative process at all - i &apos;m not the channel!
i am a mule . in order to work i have to get up and sweat every day at the same time , struggling and really uncomfortable .
but even i in my mule type have sometimes touched this thing .
and i guess many of you did that too .
even i had work or ideas that came through from a source that i really can not identify with .
what is this thing?
and how do we relate to it without losing our mind , but , on the contrary , even keeping our mental health?
for me , the best living example of how to do this is the musician tom waits . i was allowed to interview him for a magazine a few years ago .
we talked about this topic . and you know what? for most of his life , tom was the embodiment of the tormented modern artist who tried to control and handle these uncontrollable creative impulses that lived in him .
but then he got older and calmer . he told me that one day he drove on an expressway in los angeles . and everything changed for him .
he was driving at high speed when he suddenly heard a small fragment of a melody that , as is often the case with inspirations , is painfully difficult to grasp . and he wants her . she is gorgeous . he lusts for it , but he can not believe it .
he does not have paper , he does not have a pen , he does not have a tape recorder .
so he begins to ascend this old oppression in him , such as &quot; i will lose this thing and will be persecuted forever by this song .
i &apos;m not good enough , i can not do it . &quot; 
but instead of panicking , he simply stopped .
he finished the whole thought process and did something completely new .
he simply looked up into the sky and said , &quot; excuse me , can not you see that i drive a car? &quot; 
&quot; do i look like i could just write a song?
if you really want to exist , come back to a better moment , if i can take care of you .
if not , somebody is tormenting someone else today .
go and torture leonard cohen . &quot; 
his whole working process changed after that .
not the work itself . it was still often as dark as ever .
but the process and the heavy oppression that surrounds him was lifted when he took the genius out of himself , where he caused nothing but annoyance and left him where he came from , so to speak . he realized that it did not have to be an internalized , tormenting thing .
there could be a peculiar , wondrous , bizarre collaboration , a kind of conversation between tom and the alien , external thing that was not quite tom .
when i heard this story , it also changed a bit the way i worked and it saved me once before .
this idea saved me when i was in the middle of the writing process of &quot; eat , pray , love&quot;  . i fell into one of those pits of doubt that we all fall into when we work on something and it does not work out . you start to think it &apos;s going to be a disaster . this will be the worst book ever written .
not only bad , but the worst of times .
i started to think i should just stamp the project .
but then i remembered how tom had spoken in the open air and i tried it .
so i raised my eyes from the manuscript and spoke my comment in the direction of an empty corner of the room .
i said aloud , &quot; listen , you thing , you and i both know , if this book is not brilliant , it &apos;s not my fault , right?
because you see , i invest everything i have . i do not have more than that .
so if you want it better , you have to show up here and keep your share of the agreement .
okay . but , if you do not  . . . you know something , then hell with it .
i will continue writing anyway , because that &apos;s my job .
and i would like to please record that i was here today for my part of the job . &quot; 
because  . . .  . . . in the end it is like this . centuries ago , in the deserts of north africa , people gathered for moonlight festivals with sacred dances and music , which were held for hours - until dawn .
these festivals have always been great because the dancers were professional and they were fantastic .
but every now and then , very rarely , something special happened . one of the actors actually became transcendental .
and i know that you know what i mean , because i know that you have all seen such a thing in your life before .
it was as if time were at a standstill and the dancer was stepping through a kind of gate . he did nothing unlike he has ever done before - 1,000 nights before . but everything came together .
and all of a sudden , he no longer appeared purely human .
it was lit from the inside and lit from below and everything lit up with a divine fire .
when something happened back then , people knew what that was . they called it by his name .
they put their hands together and began to sing: &quot; allah , allah , allah , god , god . &quot; 
that &apos;s god , you know?
interesting historical footnote: when the moons conquered southern spain , they brought with them this need . over the centuries , the pronunciation changed from &quot; allah , allah , allah&quot;  to &quot; olé , olé , olé&quot;  , which is still heard in bullfights and flamenco dances .
if a performer in spain did something impossible , something magical: &quot; allah , olé , olé , allah , great , bravo&quot;  unbelievable , there it is: a glimpse of god .
and that &apos;s great because we need that .
but , the tricky part follows the next morning , when the dancer himself wakes up and realizes that it &apos;s 11 o &apos;clock on a tuesday - and he no longer carries a bit of god in him .
he is just an aging mortal with aching knees . and maybe he will never rise again to those heights .
and maybe someone will never call god &apos;s name again when he turns . but what should he do with the rest of his life?
that is hard  .
this is one of the most painful corrections to a creative life .
but maybe it does not have to be so full of pain if from the beginning you did not believe that the most extraordinary aspects of your existence came from them .
if you may just believe that they are a loan - of an unimaginable source for a select time of their lives , which will be passed on to someone else when they are done with it .
when we start thinking about it , it changes everything .
that &apos;s how i started to think , and i think so for the past few months while working on the book that will be released soon - as the dangerous , frightening , highly anticipated follower of my freakish success .
what i have to constantly tell myself , if i go crazy about it , is: do not be afraid .
do not be discouraged .
just do your job .
be there for your contribution , whatever it may be .
if your job is dancing , dance .
when the stupid divine genius that is on your side decides to show a touch of wonder through your effort for a brief moment , then &quot; olé! &quot; 
and if not , still dance your dance .
and &quot; olé! &quot; for you , despite all this .
i believe in it and i feel we should teach it .
&quot; olé! &quot; for you in spite of all this , just for the fact that you love the mere human and the stubbornness to be there for it .
thank you  .
thank you  .
olé!

i would like to ask you to think back to the time of your childhood , when you were playing with bricks .
as they realized how to stretch out and access the hands that absorb and move clogs , they actually learned to think and solve problems by understanding and changing spatial relationships .
spatial thinking is closely linked to how we understand a large part of our environment .
as a computer scientist - inspired by the nature of our interaction with real objects - along with my mentor patti and my colleague jeevan kalanithi , i began to wonder: what if we were to use computers instead of a mouse pointer that looks like a mouse digital fingertip moving on a flat work surface  . . .  . . . what if we could intervene with both hands and physically grab data and rearrange it the way we want it?
this question was so poignant that we decided to research the answer and develop siftables .
in short , a siftable is an interactive computer the size of a cookie .
they can be moved by hand , they can perceive each other , they can capture their movements and they have a screen and a radio receiver .
much more important: they are physical , which is why you can move them like building bricks just by reaching out and grabbing hands .
and the siftables are an example of a new generation of tools for manipulating digital data .
and as these tools become more physical , develop awareness of their movements , recognize each other and perceive the subtleties of our dealings with them , we can begin exploring new and entertaining types of interaction .
i start with a few simple examples .
this siftable is configured for video playback and if i tilt it in one direction , the video will be played forward; if i tilt it in the other direction , it runs backwards .
and these interactive portraits are mutually true .
so if i put them next to each other , that arouses their interest .
when they are surrounded by one another , they notice that too and can get a bit nervous .
and you feel your movements and inclination as well .
an interesting sequence of interactions that we began to realize was that we could apply everyday gestures to data , such as pouring paint as you do with liquids .
in this case , we configured three siftables as color pots and i can use them to add color to the middle , where they are then mixed .
if we overdo it , we can pour back a little .
there are also some nice opportunities in education such as languages , math or logic games , where we give the people the possibility of quick trial and error and want to see the results immediately .
here i am  . . . this is a fibonacci sequence that i create with a simple equation program .
this is a game of words that is a mixture of scrabble and boggle .
basically you get every round randomly assigned siftable letters on each siftable and while trying to form words , these are checked by a dictionary .
then , after about 30 seconds , it will be mixed again and you will have a new combination of spells and new possibilities to try out .
many thanks  .
these are some children who came on an excursion to the media lab . i was able to get them to try it out and recorded a video of it .
they really loved it .
an interesting feature of this type of application is that you do not have to give the people many instructions .
all you have to say is  &quot; make words &quot; and they know exactly what to do .
here are a few more people testing it .
this is our latest beta tester down there right .

the last example for which i have time to show it today is a live music sequencer that we have recently developed , where siftables acts as notes , like lead vocals , bass and drums .
each has four different variations; you can choose which one you want to use .
and you can feed these sounds into the sequence , which in turn you can arrange in any pattern .
and you feed them by simply holding a sound siftable to a sequence siftable .
there are effects that you can change live , such as . hall and filter .
you connect it with a certain tone and then tend to adjust it .
and then higher-level effects such as tempo and volume , which apply to the entire sequence .
so let &apos;s have a look .
we start by feeding the lead vocal into two sequence siftables and arranging them in a row , expanding them and adding a bit more to the voice .
now i add a bass .
now i will do some more drums .
and now i connect the filter with the drums , so i can control the effect live .
i can accelerate the whole sequence by tilting the tempo in one direction or the other .
and now i connect the filter with the bass; for something more expression .
i can change the sequence as it runs .
i do not have to plan it in advance , but can improvise; to lengthen or shorten them during this time .
and finally i can fade out the whole sequence by tilting the volume siftable to the left .
