the work of the interpreter .
music as language .
in the description to my speech it is written that i understand as an interpreter , me as a translator , so in the english word meaning .
interpreters are translators of written music into sounding music .
that as a translator not only word by word translates the text into another language , but also has to understand the deeper meaning of the words and word context , so that it makes a meaningful translation , knows anyone who has to do with foreign languages .
translating from german into english can require more knowledge , especially when it comes to poetry or even poetry .
but it is completely alien for the amateur what we musicians do with our musical language .
as an interpreter of a piece of music , i initially only have the written notes .
you are sure that the composer wrote down in the notes exactly what he thought up .
in fact , the notes are very vague and inaccurate .
these notes are about as accurate or inaccurate as a verbal , albeit very seemingly meticulous , description of a landscape .
because in this verbal landscape description , the fragrance of the flowers is not to smell , the degree of green only to guess , the murmur of the wind not to hear , not to feel .
that is , the interpreter must translate these inaccuracies of musical notation into another , audible , concrete , nonverbal musical language .
today i want to translate what i otherwise interpret musically , that is to say , into a verbal language .
in practice a translation of the translation .
therefore , i prefer to do all this in german , because if i had to translate it into english again , it would be synonymous with translation of the translation of the translation for me .
so , the music works for me so much like the spoken language .
the notated notes have the function of the letters of a font .
so it is not enough to just learn to read the notes and to press the corresponding keys on the piano or the right spot on a violin string .
those are just the letters that you have learned to paint .
you only recognize the meaning of the letters when you can speak the language .
consequently , for the learning process of the music musical language is at least as long as for the spoken language .
there are also gradations , whether you learn it for home use , just passive understands or you can also speak actively , to the musical native speaker .
the artist &apos;s work begins in a similar way to that of a theater director .
after you have chosen to play a piece , because you are enthusiastic about it , you first search for backgrounds of the genesis of the composition .
one collects information about the piece in the secondary literature .
one reads in the letters , diaries , notes , or whether there are words , thoughts of the composer himself .
one studies the works he composed before and after .
one compares .
reasons to write it .
how and what did he feel?
what were his living conditions like those of society and politics?
i &apos;m interested in everything .
one thinks oneself into this time by , for example , looking at contemporary pictures or reading literature .
in time , as an interpreter , you also have a wealth of knowledge about the composer you play more often , and it is always easier for you to think and feel in it .
the composer becomes more and more the friend with whom one lives together .
his desire to write this piece is increasingly becoming my personal concern for playing it .
since the notes are anything but an exact record of the composer &apos;s concern , but only a scaffolding , i now apply this framework to fill it again with meaning and meaning .
i then say in my own words what might be written between this framework .
i explain what these sounds could mean .
now for me and for the composer back then and what they could mean for him today .
every sound , every phrase gets a meaning , because i feel it myself , because it springs from my now personally lived .
it must be in harmony for me here and now , otherwise i can not convince my audience that this is my concern .
it is always a very happy concert , if i may have the experience , the piece that i have worked so long in advance , how to experience new .
how to experience a happy new day again and again , although always with the same rituals of life , getting up , having breakfast , going to work , so i experience the same piece again and again .
i grew older one day , the seasons change , my pulse slows down in the evening than in the morning , in a concert hall it takes more time for the sound to come back than at home .
i perceive the breathing of the audience , consciously or unconsciously .
all of this influences me and all of this also influences my experience with the composition .
it &apos;s also wonderful when i &apos;m on stage with a duo partner and we have the same wavelength .
then the same thing happens as in a good , lively conversation .
one brings in a new idea and immediately it is answered with a reaction .
it &apos;s exciting , because then things happen that we did not rehearse and what you can not rehearse .
it &apos;s a lot of fun to make music that way , and you as the audience have experienced how the music communicates alive .
so every concert is a different concert .
i &apos;ll never play like this anymore .
i can not play like i did in twenty years .
i will have more experience of life than today and therefore i will have a lot more nuances of color , emotions and feelings in my range of music sounds than today .
sure , i &apos;ll throw some pots out , but get new ones for that .

when steve lopez , los angeles times columnist , one day walked the streets in the center of los angeles , he heard a wonderful music .
she came from a man , an african-american , sympathetic , rough , homeless , who played on a violin that had only two strings left .
many of them will know the story , because steve &apos;s article later became a book that was filmed , with robert downey junior as steve lopez and jamie foxx as nathaniel anthony ayers , the contrabassist , who studied at the juilliard conservatory and its promising tragically ended early by his paranoid schizophrenia .
nathaniel left juilliard , suffered a nervous breakdown , and 30 years later he was homeless on the streets of skid row in the center of los angeles .
i recommend you all to read steve &apos;s book or to watch the movie , so that you not only understand the wonderful bond that has developed between these two men , but also how the music helped to make this connection and how it was ultimately the instrument . if this pun is allowed , helping to get nathaniel off the street .
i met mr ayers in 2008 , two years ago , in the walt disney concert hall .
he had just heard a performance of beethoven &apos;s first and fourth symphony and came backstage to introduce himself to me .
he spoke in a very cheerful and sociable tone about yo-yo ma and hillary clinton and about how the dodgers would never make it into the baseball world series . and all because of the treacherous passage of the first violin in the last movement of beethoven &apos;s fourth symphony .
we came to the topic of music . and a few days later i got an e-mail stating that nathaniel was interested in violin lessons for me .
i must also mention that nathaniel refused medical treatment because he had already been treated with electric shocks and with thorazin and handcuffs , a trauma that has persecuted him all his life .
as a result , he is now particularly vulnerable to these schizophrenic phases . sometimes they are so bad and he disappears for days , wandering the streets of skid row , always exposed to the horror and torture of his own mind .
and that &apos;s how nathaniel was when we started our first lesson in the walt disney concert hall . he had this mad flickering in his eyes and seemed completely lost .
he talked about invisible demons and smokes and about how someone wanted to poison him in his sleep .
i was scared , not because of me , but i was afraid that i might lose him , that he might sink into one of his states , and that i could destroy his relationship with the violin when i began , via scales and arpeggios and other exciting forms didactic violin pedagogy .
so i just started to play .
i played the first movement of beethoven &apos;s violin concerto .
and as i played , it struck me that there was a complete change in nathaniel &apos;s eyes .
it was as if he were under the influence of an invisible medicine , a chemical reaction whose catalyst was my game .
nathaniel &apos;s manic wrath turned into understanding , into a quiet curiosity and grace .
and , as if by a miracle , he took his violin and began to play some excerpts of violin concertos and then asked me to play them to the end: mendelssohn , tchaikovsky , sibelius .
we then began to talk about music , from bach to beethoven , brahms , bruckner and all the other b &apos;s , from bartok to esa-pekka salonen .
and i realized that not only did he have an encyclopedic knowledge of music , but he also had a close personal relationship with it .
he spoke of her with a passion and an understanding that i otherwise only know from my colleagues in the los angeles philharmonic .
by playing music and talking about music , this paranoid , confused man , who had just passed the streets of los angeles , had become a lovable , educated , excellent musician trained in juilliard .
music is medicine . music changes us .
for nathaniel , music means spiritual health .
because the music allows him to transform his thoughts and delusions into something real with the help of his imagination and creativity .
and so he escapes his tormenting states .
i understood that this is the essence of art .
that &apos;s why we make music: to make something that is deep inside all of us , our emotions , through our artistic lens , through our creativity into reality .
and the reality of that expression reaches us all , and moves , inspires and unites us .
as far as nathaniel was concerned , the music brought him back into a community of friends .
the redeeming power of music brought him back to a family of musicians who understood him , who recognized his talent and respected him .
and i will play with nathaniel again and again , whether in the walt disney concert hall or skid row , because it reminds me why i have become a musician .
many thanks  .
bruno giussani: thank you . thank you  .

it could be said that it is a recipe for the future of good food , whether we are talking about bass or cattle .
what we need now is a radically new concept of agriculture , one in which food really tastes good .
but for a lot of people this is a bit too radical .
we are not realists , we enjoy . we are lovers .
we love weekly markets . we love small family businesses . we talk about regional foods . we eat organic .
and if you say these are the things that assure the future of good food , someone stands up somewhere and says , &quot; hey people , i love pink flamingos , but how are you going to feed the world?
how will you feed the world?  &quot;
may i be honest ?
i do not like this question .
no , not because we already produce enough calories to feed more than just the world .
a billion people will starve today .
a billion - more than ever before - due to gross inequalities in distribution , not total production .
well , i do not like this question because it has determined the logic of our food system for the last 50 years .
feed crops to herbivores , pesticides to monocultures , chemicals to the earth , chicken to fish , and all the while the agribusiness has simply asked , &quot; if we feed more people cheaper , how dreadful can that be? &quot;
that was the motivation . it was the justification . it was the business plan of american agriculture .
we should name her , what she is , a business in progress , a business that quickly eats up ecological capital that makes this production possible .
this is not a trade and it is not agriculture .
our grain chamber is threatened today , not because of decreasing supply , but because of diminishing resources,
not by the latest combines and tractor inventions , but by fertile land , not by pumping , but by fresh water , not by chainsaws , but by forests , and not by fishing boats and nets , but by fish in the sea .
you want to feed the world?
let &apos;s start with the question: how will we feed ourselves?
or better , how can we create conditions that enable every community to feed themselves?
to do that , do not look at the agricultural industry model for the future .
it &apos;s really old , and it &apos;s done .
capital , chemistry , and machinery are in the lead , and it never produced anything really good for food .
let &apos;s look at the ecological model instead .
this is the one that draws on two billion years of work experience .
look at miguel , farmers like miguel,
plants that are not worlds apart , plants that restore rather than use up , plants that grow extensively rather than just intensive , farmers who are not just producers , but experts in relationships,
because they are the ones who are also experts for taste .
and if i &apos;m honest , you &apos;re a better cook than i &apos;ll ever be .
you know , that &apos;s fine with me , because if that is the future of good food , it will be delicious .

i grew up with a good portion of science fiction .
during my school days , i took the bus to school , one hour per route every day .
and my nose was always in a book , a science-fiction book , that kidnapped my thoughts into other worlds and narrated my deep-seated , insatiable curiosity in a narrative way .
this curiosity was also reflected in the fact that whenever i was out of school i used to walk , wander and &quot; rehearse&quot;  through the forests; collected , frogs and snakes and beetles and pond water , brought everything home and looked at it under the microscope .
i was totally enthusiastic about science , you know .
it was always about the attempt to understand the world and explore the limits of the possible .
and my love of science fiction seemed to be reflected in my environment , because back in the late  &apos;60s , we flew to the moon and explored the deep sea .
jacques cousteau came to our living room with its fascinating programs that showed us animals and places and a world full of wonders that we had never imagined before .
that probably went very well with the science-fiction-role .
and i was an artist .
i could draw . i could paint .
and since there were no video games nor this oversaturation of computer-generated films nor the whole visual language in the media landscape , i had to create the images in my head .
we all had to do that at the time . when we as children read a book , we took the author &apos;s description and projected it on the screen in our heads .
my reaction was to draw and paint extraterrestrial , alien worlds , robots , spaceships and all that .
in math lessons i was constantly caught by the teacher , as i , hiding behind the textbook , painted around .
the creativity had to get out somehow .
and something interesting happened . what fascinated me about jacques cousteaus broadcasts was the thought of a completely unknown world here on our earth itself .
surely i would never reach an alien world with a spaceship . that seemed pretty unlikely to me .
but here was a world that i could actually enter , here on earth , and it was as fascinating and exotic as anything i had ever imagined when i read these books .
so , when i was 15 , i decided to become a diver .
the only problem was that i lived in a small village in canada , 1 000 km from the nearest ocean .
but i did not let that discourage me .
i annoyed my father until he finally found a diving school in buffalo , new york , just across the border where we lived .
i made my diving license in a pool of the ymca in the middle of winter in buffalo , new york .
but the ocean , a real ocean , came to my face only two years later when we moved to california .
since then , in the 40 years that have passed , i spent about 3,000 hours under water . 500 hours of it in diving boats .
and i &apos;ve learned that the world of the deep sea , even in the shallower oceans , is as rich in fascinating life as we can barely imagine .
nature &apos;s imagination knows no boundaries , unlike our own pitiful human imagination .
to this day , i feel the deepest awe for what i see on my dives .
and my love for the ocean continues , with the same intensity as ever .
when i chose a job as an adult , it was filmmaking .
that seemed the best way to tell my inner urge to tell stories , with my need to create and unite images .
as a kid , i kept drawing comic books and stuff .
so movies were the way to bring pictures and stories together . that matched .
of course , the stories i picked out were from the science fiction section: &quot; terminator &quot;  , &quot; aliens &quot;  , and  &quot; abyss &quot; ,
at  &quot; abyss &quot; i was able to combine my love for the underwater world and diving with filmmaking .
so almost my two passions intertwined .
something interesting happened with  &quot; abyss &quot; : in order to solve a narrative problem in this film - and we had to create such a kind of liquid water creature - we resorted to computer generated animation , cg .
what came out of it was the first computer-generated soft-surface figure ever seen in a movie .
although the film did not bring in any money , in fact , it was just recording its production costs , but i noticed something intriguing: audiences all over the world were mesmerized by the magic that came from it .
according to arthur clarke  &apos;; it is a well-known fact that it is no longer possible to distinguish advanced technology and magic from one another .
so they saw something magical .
and i found that incredibly exciting .
and i thought to myself: &quot; wow , that must necessarily be included in the film art . &quot;
so we went in &quot; terminator 2 &quot;  , my next film , much further .
together with ilm we created the types of liquid metal there . the success depended entirely on how this effect would arrive .
and it worked  . again we had created something magical . and the effect in the audience was the same . however , we have already recorded a bit more money with the film .
starting from these two experiences , a whole new world has been reached , a whole new world of creativity for filmmakers .
so i started a company together with my good friend stan winston , who was the best make-up and creature designer at that time . it was called  &quot; digital domain &quot; ,
the basic idea of ​​this company was to skip the phase of analogue processes with optical printers etc . and to start with digital productions right away .
we did that as well and gained a competitive advantage for a while .
but in the middle of the nineties we realized that we were moving too slowly in the creature and character design - for which we had actually founded the company .
i had this piece &quot; avatar &quot; written to raise the bar to new heights in terms of visual effects and computer-generated effects , with realistic human expressive characters created using cg . and the main characters should all be cg . and the world should be cg .
but the bar pushed back pretty well . and the people in my company told me that for the time being we are not able to do that .
so i moved that and made this other movie about the big ship going down .
i think of the filmstudio as  &quot; romeo and julia on a ship &quot; sold . it became a love movie of epic proportions , a passionate film .
secretly , however , i really wanted to dive down to the real wreck of the titanic .
that &apos;s why i made the film .
that &apos;s the truth  . the studio did not know that .
but i convinced them by saying , &quot; we dive to the wreck , we film the real wreck .
we will show it in the opening sequence of the film .
that is very important . it is a good hanger for marketing .  &quot;
and i persuaded her to finance an expedition .
sounds crazy . but that goes back to the fact that your own imagination can create a reality .
in fact , six months later , we created a reality in which i found myself in a russian dive boat , four kilometers below the surface of the north atlantic , and watched the real titanic through a bullseye .
that was not a movie , no hd , that was real .
well , that really blew me away .
and the preparations were enormous . we had to build cameras and headlights and everything .
and i noticed how much these deep-sea dives resembled a space mission .
well , they were also highly technical and required extensive planning .
you climb into this capsule , then you float down into this dark , hostile environment , where there is no hope of salvation unless you can do it yourself .
and i thought , &quot; wow , that &apos;s just like being in a science fiction movie .
that is really cool  .  &quot;
i was obsessed with exploring the deep sea .
in any case , of the part that has to do with curiosity and science . it was all  . it was adventure , it was curiosity . it was imagination .
and it was an experience hollywood could not give me .
because , you know , i could imagine a being for which we could then create a visual effect . but i could not imagine what i would see outside the window .
on some of the following expeditions , i have seen beings in thermal springs , and sometimes things i &apos;ve never seen before , sometimes things that no one had ever seen before , and science at the time we saw them no words found .
that really upset me , and i wanted more .
and so i made a somewhat unusual decision .
after the success of  &quot; titanic &quot; i said to myself , &quot; alright , i want to put my full-time job as a filmmaker in hollywood on ice and become a full-time researcher for a while . &quot;
and then we started , we dived to &quot; bismarck &quot;  , and explored them with robotic vehicles .
we returned to the wreck of the titanic .
we took small robots that we had built , which unfolded a fiberglass cable .
our intention was to dive in and look at the interior of the ship , which had never been done before .
nobody had ever explored the interior of the wreck . one had no means for it . so we developed the necessary technology .
now and then , sitting on the deck of the titanic , in a dive boat , i see planks that are quite similar to those boards here , and that i know once the band played there .
and i fly with a small robot vehicle through the corridors of the ship .
i only use it , but my consciousness is inside the vehicle .
it felt like i was physically present within the shipwreck of the titanic .
that was the most surreal form of déjà-vu i have ever experienced . because before i turned around each corner , i always knew what would appear in the same light cone of the vehicle , since i had run over the film set for months , when we turned the film .
the set was an exact copy of the ship &apos;s blueprints .
so that was a very strange experience .
she made me realize this telepresence experience that you can use such robotic avatars and so shift your own consciousness into the vehicle , into this other form of existence .
it really was profoundly profound
and maybe even a small outlook on what might happen in a few decades , if you have cyborg bodies to do research or do other things , in all the possible post-human future scenarios that i think of as science-fiction fan can introduce .
after these expeditions , we really began to appreciate what we saw down there , such as those deep-sea springs where we saw these incredibly baffling animals . these are quasi alien , but here on earth .
they live with the help of chemosynthesis .
they do not exist in a sunlight-based system as we do that .
and so you see animals that are next to a 500 degrees celsius hot that they can survive there .
at the same time i was also very interested in space science , that was again the science fiction influence of my childhood .
and so i ended up with the people who are interested in space and who are dealing with nasa and sitting in the nasa council of experts and planning real space missions . you go to russia , go through the biomedical protocol before the mission , including all the trimmings , then fly to the international space station and take our 3d camera systems with you .
that was fascinating .
but in the end , it came down to me taking space scientists with us into the deep sea .
i took them with me and gave them access to the world below: astrobiologists , planet scientists , people who were interested in such extreme situations . i took them to the springs so they could look around , sample and test instruments , and so on .
we made documentary films , but we actually did science , more precisely: space science .
so the circle between my existence as a science fiction fan , then as a child , and the implementation into the reality closed .
in the course of this expedition , i learned a lot .
i learned a lot about science . but now i think you certainly , a director must also be able to lead a leader like a ship &apos;s captain or something .
but i did not understand much of leadership before i started these expeditions .
because at one point i had to tell myself: &quot; what am i doing here?
why do i do that? what happens?  &quot;
these stupid movies do not bring us any money .
we &apos;re just recording the production costs . no trace of fame .
everyone thinks i am between &quot; titanic &quot; and &quot; avatar &quot; ran away and nailed my nails on a towel on the beach somewhere .
i have made all these films , these documentary films , for a very small audience .
no fame , no honor , no money . what are you doing ?
you do it for the sake of the task , for the sake of challenge - and the ocean is the most challenging environment there is . you make it because of the explorer noise , and because of the strange connection that arises when a small group of people forms a team .
because we did all this with only 10 to 12 people who worked together for years without interruption . sometimes we were at sea for 2 to 3 months in a row .
and in this community , you realize that the most important thing is the respect you have for one another , because you have mastered a task that you can not explain to anyone else .
when one returns to land and says , &quot; we had to do it , the fiberglass , the sound attenuation , and all around it , all the engineering , and the difficulties , the human capacity to work at sea &quot;  , then you just can not explain the other one . it &apos;s like policemen or soldiers who have gone through something together and know that they can never explain it to anyone .
there arises a connection , a mutual respect .
so when i came back to my next movie &quot; avatar &quot; i tried to use the same style of leadership , that of respecting your team and respecting it in return .
that really changed the dynamics .
so there i was again with a small team on unknown terrain . we turned &quot; avatar &quot; with a new technology that did not exist before .
tremendously exciting .
a tremendous challenge .
and we became a family for four and a half years .
this changed my way of making films completely .
there were people who thought we had mimicked these ocean creatures really well and transported them to the planet pandora .
for me it was more of a principled way to do my job , the actual process that has changed in the result .
so what can we conclude from all this?

today i talk to you about energy and climate .
and that may be surprising given that my full-time employment at the foundation is mainly about vaccines and seeds , about the things we need to invent and deliver to enable the poorest 2 billion people to live a better life .
but energy and climate are extremely important to these people , in fact even more important than anyone else on the planet .
climate deterioration means that their crops will not grow over many years . we rain too much or too little . things will change as their fragile environment can not stand .
that leads to hunger dying . it leads to uncertainty . it leads to unrest .
so , a climate change will be terrible for them .
in addition , the energy price is very important to them .
the fact is that if you could only reduce the price by one thing , reducing energy would be by far the most effective way of reducing poverty .
well , the energy price has fallen over time .
in fact , the progressive society is based on energy advances .
the coal revolution drove the industrial revolution and even in the 20th century there was a rapid fall in the price of electricity and that &apos;s why we have refrigerators , air conditioners , we can make modern materials and do so many things .
so we are in a wonderful situation with electricity in the rich world .
but , if we reduce the price - let &apos;s cut the price in half - we hit a new barrier and this barrier is related to co2 .
co2 warms the planet and the equation for co2 is actually pretty clear .
they add up the expelled co2 , which leads to the temperature increase and this temperature increase has some very negative consequences . effects on the weather and perhaps worse , the indirect consequence that natural ecosystems can not adapt to such rapid changes and thus complete systems collapse .
well , the exact connection between a co2 increase and the resulting temperature change and where their further consequences are , there are some ambiguities , but not very many .
and there is no doubt how bad these consequences will be . but they will be extremely bad!
i &apos;ve asked the top scientists several times: &quot; do we really have to come down to near zero?
is not enough half or a quarter?  &quot;
the answer is: until we come close to zero , the temperature will continue to rise .
that &apos;s why it &apos;s a big challenge .
it is very different from saying that we have a 3.5 m high truck that has to come under a 3 m bridge . you can squeeze it almost under it .
this one has to go all the way down to zero .
well , every year we emit a lot of carbon dioxide , over 26 billion tons .
each american about 20 tons . people from poor countries , less than one .
on average , there are about 5 tons for everyone on the planet .
and somehow we have to bring about changes that bring it down to zero .
it has been rising constantly .
only different economic changes have influenced it at all and we have to go from a rapid increase to a reduction and a reduction to zero .

so let &apos;s take a look at each factor and think about how to get it to zero .
probably one of these numbers must come very close to zero .

a few years ago , here at ted , peter skillman created a design contest called &quot; the marshmallow challenge &quot; in front  .
the idea is pretty simple . teams of four need to build the largest possible freestanding structure with 20 spaghetti , approx . 1m adhesive tape , approx . 1m thread and a marshmallow .
the marshmallow must be on top .
and , although it seems really easy , it is actually really hard because it forces people to work together very quickly .
and so i thought that this is an interesting idea and i turned it into a design workshop .
it was a huge success .
since then , i &apos;ve run about 70 workshops worldwide , with students , developers , and architects , even ctos from the fortune 50 companies , and there &apos;s something about this task that gives a deep sense of the nature of the collaboration , and i want to share some of it with them ,
usually , most people start to orient themselves on the task .
they talk about it , they think about what it &apos;s going to look like , they &apos;re fighting for power,
then invest a little time in planning and organization . they sketch and put out spaghetti .
they invest a great deal of their time in the construction of constantly growing structures
and finally , just before you run out of time , someone takes the marshmallow and they gently set it on the top , step back and &quot; ta-da &quot; ! admire your work .
but what happens then , almost always , is that the  &quot; ta-da &quot; to a  &quot; uh-oh &quot; because the weight of the marshmallow causes the whole thing to bend and collapse .
there are a number of people who are much more  &quot; uh-oh &quot; -momentes have more than others , and among the worst are fresh bwl graduates .
they lie , they cheat , they are confused and produce really scanty things .
and of course , there are teams that are much more &quot; ta-da &quot; - have structures and among the best are fresh graduates of the kindergarten .
and that &apos;s pretty amazing .
as peter told us , they produce not only the highest towers , but also the most interesting structures of all .
what you might ask is: how come? why ? what &apos;s up with them ?
and peter tells us: &quot; none of the children invest time in being the boss of spaghetti gmbh &quot; ,
they do not invest time in power struggles .
but there is another reason .
namely that bwl students were trained to find the right plan , ok!
and then realize it .
what happens then , when you take the marshmallow to the extreme , you have no more time and what happens?
it is a crisis .
sounds familiar , what?
what kindergarten children do differently is that they start with the marshmallow and build prototypes , successful prototypes , always with the marshmallow at the top , so that they have several times the opportunity to repair dilapidated prototypes .
designers recognize this kind of cooperation as the core of an iterative process .
and with every attempt , the children get instant feedback which works and what does not .
that &apos;s why the power to work with prototypes is essential - but let &apos;s see how different teams operate .
the average for most is about 50cm , bwl students manage half of it , lawyers a little more , but not much , kindergarten children are better than the adults .
who is the most successful?
architects and engineers , fortunately .
1m is the highest i &apos;ve seen .
and why ? because they understand triangles and self-reinforcing geometric patterns are the key to building stable structures .
ceos are slightly above average . but here it gets interesting .
if you put an executive administrator on the team , they will be significantly better .
it &apos;s incredible  . they look around and realize , &quot; this team will win . &quot;
they can predict that . why is that possible?
because they have special process support skills .
they guide the process , they understand it .
and the team that governs and pays attention to the work will significantly improve the performance of the team .
specialized skills and process skills and their combination lead to success .
if you have 10 teams that are typical , you get about 6 , which have stable structures .
i tried something exciting .
i thought , we bring the pokereinsatz the same .
so i offered a $ 10,000 prize to software for the winners .
what do you think happened with these design students?
what was the result ?
that happened here . not a team had a stable structure .
if anyone had built a 2.5cm high construction , he would have carried the price home .
so is not it interesting that high bets have a strong effect?
we repeated this exercise with the same students .
what do you think happened?
now they understood the advantage of &quot; prototyping &quot; ,
so the same bad team became one of the best .
they produced the highest construction in the least amount of time .
there are profound lessons for us , about the nature of drive and success .
you might ask: why would anyone actually invest time to create a marshmallow challenge?
the bottom line is , i help create digital tools and processes to help teams , cars , video games and &quot; visual effects &quot; to create  .
and what the marshmallow challenge does is help you identify hidden assumptions .
because frankly , every project has its own marshmallow .
the challenge is to have a shared experience , a common language or attitude , to build the right prototype .
and this is the value of this experience , this simple exercise .
and those of you who are interested can visit marshmallowchallenge .com .
it &apos;s a blog where you can see how to build the marshmallows .
there is a step-by-step guide there .
you will find crazy examples from all over the world how people optimize and refine the system .
there are also world records .
and the basic lesson , i think , is that construction is actually a unifying sport .
it demands that we all direct our senses to the task and that we use our thinking optimally , as our emotions and ours do , in the challenge that lies ahead .
and sometimes a small prototype of this experience is all it takes us from a  &quot; uh-oh &quot; - to a  &quot; ta-da &quot; -moment to bring .
and that can make a big difference .

everyone is talking about happiness these days .
i have some people count the number of books that are labeled &quot; happiness &quot; have been published in the title over the last five years and they gave up after about 40 , and there were many more .
there is a tremendous wave of interest in happiness among researchers .
there is a lot of luck coaching .
everyone would like to make people happier .
but despite all this torrent of work , there are several cognitive traps that make it almost impossible to think clearly about happiness .
and my talk today will mainly be about these cognitive traps .
this is true for lay people who think about their own happiness , and it applies to scientists who think about happiness because it turns out that we are just as confused as anyone else .
the first of these falls is a reluctance to admit complexity .
it turns out that the word happiness is simply not a useful word because we apply it to too many different things .
i think there is a definite meaning to which we could limit it , but by and large it is something we have to give up , and we will have to take the complicated view of what is wellbeing .
the second trap is a confusion of experience and memory: basically , she is between being happy in his life and being happy about his life or happy with his life .
and these are two very different concepts , and they are both thrown together in the idea of ​​happiness .
and the third is the concentration illusion , and that is the unfortunate fact that we can not think about a circumstance that affects well-being without distorting its meaning .
i mean , that &apos;s a real cognitive trap .
there &apos;s just no way to get it right .
now i would like to start with an example from someone who had a question and answer session after one of my lectures that told a story . [indistinctly  . . .] he said he had heard a symphony , and it was absolutely magnificent music , and at the very end of the recording there was a terrible screeching sound .
and then he added , really emotional , that ruined the whole experience .
but that did not happen .
what it had ruined were the memories of the experience .
he had the experience .
he had had 20 minutes of wonderful music .
they did not count because he remained with a memory; the memory was ruined and the memory was all he had to keep .
what this really means to us is that we could think about ourselves and other people in the sense of two kinds of self .
there is an experiencer himself who lives in the present and knows the present , is capable of reliving the past , but basically has only the present .
it is the experiencing itself that appeals to the doctor - you know when the doctor asks: &quot; does it hurt now if i touch her here? &quot;
and then there is a reminding itself , and the reminding itself is that which counts and upholds the story of our lives , and it is the one that appeals to the physician by asking the question: &quot; how have you felt lately ?  &quot;
or &quot; how was your trip to albania? &quot; or something like that  .
these are two very different entities , the experiencing self and the remembering self , and to confuse the two is part of the confusion of the concept of happiness .
well , the remembering itself is a story teller .
and that really starts with a fundamental reaction to our memories - it starts right away .
we do not just tell stories when we set out to tell stories .
our memory tells us stories , that is , what we are allowed to keep from our experiences is a story .
and let &apos;s not start with an example .
there is an old study .
these are real patients who undergo a painful procedure .
i will not go into detail . it is not painful these days , but it was painful when this study was done in the 1990s .
they were asked to report their pain every 60 seconds .
and here are two patients . these are your records .
and they are asked , &quot; which of them has suffered more?&quot; 
and that is a very simple question .
patient b has clearly suffered more . his colonoscopy was longer , and every minute of pain that patient had a had patient b , and more .
but now there is another question: &quot; how much did these patients think they were suffering? &quot;
and here is a surprise:
and the surprise is that patient a had a much worse reminder of colonoscopy than patient b .
the stories of the bowels were different and because a very crucial part of the story is how it ends -
and none of these stories is very edifying or great - but one of them is this clearly  . . .
but one of them is significantly worse than the other .
and the one worse is where the pain was at its very high end . it &apos;s a bad story .
how do we know this?
because we asked these people for their bowels , and also much later: &quot; how bad was the whole thing in general? &quot;  .
and she was far worse for a than for b in the recollection .
well , that &apos;s a direct conflict between the experiencing self and the remembering self .
from the perspective of the experiencer himself , b clearly had a worse time .
well , what you could do with patient a , and we &apos;ve actually done clinical experiments , and it &apos;s been done , and it works , you could actually extend the colonoscopy of patient a by simply letting the tube in without too much of it shake .
this will cause the patient to suffer , but only a little and much less than before .
and if you do that for a couple of minutes , it has the effect that the patient himself is worse off , and the reminding himself of patient a is much better off , because now patient a has a better story given his experience .
what makes a story different?
and that applies to the stories that memory provides us , and it also applies to the stories we invent .
what makes a story stand out are upheavals , significant moments and ends .
endings are very , very important and in this case the end dominated .
now , the experiencer himself lives his life continuously .
it has moments of experience , one after the other .
and you ask: what happens to those moments?
and the answer is really simple . they are lost forever .
i mean , most of the moments of our lives - and i figured that out - you know , the psychological present is about three seconds long . which means you know that in one lifetime there are about 600 million of them . in one month there are about 600,000 . most of them leave no trace .
most of them are completely ignored by reminding themselves .
and yet somehow they get the impression that they should count that what happens during these moments of experience is our life .
it is the limited resource that we consume while we are in this world .
and how we consume them would seem to be important , but that &apos;s not the story that remembers even for us .
so we have the self remembering and the experiencing ourselves , and they are really quite different .
the biggest difference between them is how to deal with time .
from the perspective of the experiencer himself , if you have a holiday and the second week is just as good as the first , then the two-week holiday is twice as good as the one-week holiday .
this does not work at all for the remembering self .
for the reminding itself , a two-week vacation is hardly better than the one-week vacation because there are no new memories added .
they did not change the story .
and in this way , time is actually the critical variable that distinguishes a remembering self from an experiencing self . time has very little influence on this story .
well , the reminding itself does more than remember and tell stories .
it is actually the one that makes decisions , because if you have a patient who , say , had two bowel reflexes at two different surgeons and decides which one to choose , then the one who chooses who has the memory is the one who chooses less bad and that is the surgeon who will be selected .
the experiencer himself has no voice in this choice .
actually , we do not choose between experiences . we choose between memories of experiences .
and even when we think about the future , we do not usually think of our future as an experience .
we think about our future as foreseen memories .
and basically , they can look at that , you know , as a tyranny of the remembering self , and they can think of the remembering self as one that , as it were , drags the experiencing itself through experiences that the experiencer himself does not need .
i have the impression that when we go on vacation that is very often the case , that is , we make holidays as much as possible in the service of reminding ourselves .
and that &apos;s a bit hard to justify , i think .
i mean , how much do we draw from our memories?
this is one of the explanations given for the dominance of the remembering self .
and when i think about it , i think of a holiday we did a couple of years ago in the antarctic , which was clearly the best vacation i &apos;ve ever had , and i think about it a lot in proportion to how many times i spend think about other holidays .
and i &apos;ve probably consumed my memories of this three-week trip , i &apos;d say , for about 25 minutes in the last four years .
well , if i had ever opened the folder with the 600 images in it i would have spent another hour .
well , that &apos;s three weeks , and that &apos;s a maximum of one and a half hours .
there seems to be a discrepancy .
well , i may be a bit extreme , you know , in how little appetite i have for the memory of memories , but even if you do more , there is a real question . why do we give memories so much weight in comparison to the weight that we give experiences?
so i want you to think about a thought experiment .
imagine your next vacation , you know that at the end of your vacation all your pictures will be destroyed and you will get an amnestic drug so that you will not remember anything .
well , would you choose the same vacation?
and if they choose another vacation , there is a conflict between their two kinds of self and they have to think about how they decide that conflict and that really is not obvious at all , because if you think in terms of time they get an answer  , and if you think in the sense of memories , you might get another answer .
why we choose the vacation that we choose is a problem that presents itself with a choice between the two types of self .
well , the two kinds of self raise two notions of happiness .
there are actually two concepts of happiness that we can apply , one per self .
so you can ask: how happy is the experiencer himself?
and then you would ask: how happy are the moments in the life of the experiencer himself?
and they are all - happiness for a moment is a rather complicated process .
what are the emotions that can be measured?
and , by the way , we are now able to get a pretty good idea of ​​the happiness of the experiencer himself over time .
if you ask for the happiness of remembering yourself , it is a very different matter .
this is not about how happy a person lives .
it &apos;s about how satisfied or pleased the person is when that person thinks about their lives .
very different concept .
anyone who does not distinguish these concepts will screw up the exploration of happiness , and i belong to a multitude of researchers of well-being who have long messed up the exploration of happiness in just that way .
the distinction between the happiness of the experiencer himself and the satisfaction of the remembering self has been recognized in recent years , and now efforts are being made to measure them separately .
the gallup organization has conducted a worldwide survey asking over half a million people about what they think about their lives and their experiences . and there were other efforts in the direction .
in recent years , we have begun to learn about the good fortune of the two kinds of self .
and the main lesson we have learned , i think , is that they are really different .
they know how satisfied someone is with their lives , and that really does not teach them much about how happy he is to live his life and vice versa .
just to give you an idea of ​​the correlation , the correlation is about  .5 .
what that means is that if you were to meet someone and you would tell them , oh , his father is a two-meter man , how much would you know about his size?
well , you would know something about its size , but there is a lot of uncertainty .
you have so much uncertainty .
when i tell you that someone has classified his life as eight on a scale of ten , you have much uncertainty about how happy he is with his experiencing himself .
the correlation is therefore low .
we know something about what determines the satisfaction of happiness-self .
we know that money is very important , goals are very important .
we know that happiness is mainly about being happy with people we like , spending time with people we like .
there are other pleasures , but that is dominant .
so if you want to maximize the happiness of the two kinds of self , you will end up doing very different things .
the conclusion from what i said here is that we really should not think of happiness as a substitute for well-being .
it is a completely different concept .
well , very briefly , another reason we can not clearly think about happiness is that we do not pay attention to the same things when we think about life and when we actually live .
so if you ask the simple question of how happy people are in california , they will not get the right answer .
when you ask that question , you think people in california need to be happier when they live , say , ohio .
and what happens is that when you think about life in california , you think about the difference between california and other places , and that difference is , let &apos;s say , climate .
well , it turns out that climate is not very important to the experiencer himself , and it is not even very important to the thoughtful self that decides how happy people are .
but now , because the thoughtful self is responsible , they may end up - some people might end up moving to california .
and it &apos;s kind of interesting to see what happens to people who move to california hoping to become happier .
well , you living yourself will not be happier .
we know that  .
but a thing will happen . they will think they are happier , because when they think about it , they will be reminded of how terrible the weather was in ohio . and you will feel that you have made the right decision .
it is very difficult to think clearly about well-being , and i hope i have given you an idea of ​​how difficult it is .

i am jane mcgigigal . i design computer games .
i &apos;ve been developing online games for 10 years now . and my goal for the next decade is to make it as easy to save the world in the real world as it is to save in online games .
i have a plan for that . i want to convince more people , including them all , to spend more time playing bigger and better games .

three billion hours a week is not nearly enough to solve the world &apos;s most pressing problems .
in fact , i am convinced that if we want to survive the next century on this planet , we need to dramatically increase that time .
i calculated that the required time is 21 billion hours of play per week .
this may seem a little outlandish at first , so i &apos;ll repeat it for it to work: if we want to solve problems such as hunger , poverty , climate change , global conflict and obesity , i think we must try to play games online for at least 21 billion hours a week to play until the end of the next decade .

my inspiration comes from an event that dates back 2,500 years .
there are these antique cubes , made of sheep &apos;s knuckles . you know ?
there were sheep &apos;s knuckles in front of these fantastic game controllers .
and these were , so to speak , the first game machines developed by humans .
and if you are familiar with the works of the ancient greek historian herodotus , you may know this story . the story of how and why games were invented .
according to herodotus , games , more precisely dice games , were invented in the kingdom of lydia during a famine .
it was said that there was such a great famine that the king of lydia decided on a crazy idea .
the people suffered . the people were fighting .
it was an extreme situation . you needed an extreme solution .
so , according to herodotus , they invented the dice game and agreed on a national strategy . one day you would eat . the next day you would play .
and they would be so absorbed in the dice game that games are so intriguing and surround us with satisfying , blissful productivity that they would forget that there was nothing to eat .
and then , the next day you would play . and the next day you would eat .
and according to herodotus , they survived this famine for 18 years by eating one day and playing the next .
likewise , i think , we play games today .
we use games to escape the suffering of the real world .
we use games to escape everything that does not work in the real world , anything that is not satisfying in real life , and we get what we need from the games .
but that does not have to be the end .
that &apos;s the exciting thing .
according to herodotus , the famine did not improve after 18 years . so the king decided to play a last dice game .
they divided the kingdom in half .
they played a dice game and the winners were allowed to embark on an epic adventure .
they left lydia and started searching for a new home . they left just as many people as there was food to survive and searched with the rest for a territory where they could thrive .
that sounds crazy , right?
but recent dna evidence shows that the etruscans , who later formed the roman empire , have the same dna as the ancient lydians .
therefore , scientists recently came to the conclusion that herodotus &apos;; crazy story is actually true .
and geologists found evidence of a global cooling that lasted nearly 20 years , which could explain the famine .
so this crazy story might be right .
perhaps they have actually saved their people by playing by escaping into games for eighteen years and then having this in fl uence and learning so much about playing together that they have thereby saved the entire civilization .

i would like to tell you something that i noticed a few months ago when writing an article for italian wired .
i always had my synonym dictionary at hand when writing , but i was already done editing the text , and i noticed that i have never looked up in my life , what the word &quot; hinders &quot; really means .
i read you the entry .
 &quot; with special needs  &quot;  , adjective: &quot; crippled , helpless , useless , destroyed , dead , mutilated , wounded , mauled , lame , severely neglected , disused , weakened , impotent , castrated , paralyzed , handicapped senil , decrepit , pulled out of traffic , repaired , done , broken broke , counted , see also hurt , useless and weak .
antonyms: healthy , strong , powerful .  &quot;
i read this list a friend aloud and had to laugh first , it seemed so ridiculous , but i only came to &quot; prepared &quot; and could not continue talking , and had to stop reading and collect myself first after this raiding word flood and the associated emotional shock .
of course that was a frayed old synonym dictionary . i just thought that the issue must be quite old .
but in fact it was an issue in the early 80s when i first started elementary school and began to build and shape my self-image outside of my family environment , also with regard to other children and the rest of the world around me .
and thank god i did not use a synonym dictionary at that time .
if i were to take this entry seriously , i would have been born into a world that someone like me would perceive as a person whose life can not be positively positive . today , however , i will be celebrating the opportunities and adventures i have experienced .
so i immediately called the online edition of 2009 and expected that i find a more appealing contribution here .
here is the updated version of this entry .
unfortunately she is not much better .
in particular , the last two words shake me under  &quot; near antonyms &quot;  &quot; all  &quot; and healthy  &quot; ,
but it &apos;s not just about the words .
it &apos;s about what we think about the people we describe with these words .
it &apos;s about the values ​​that are in these words , and how we construct those values .
our language influences our thinking and how we see the world and the people around us .
many ancient societies , including the greeks and the romans , really believed that the pronunciation of a curse has a great power , because what is said aloud can also manifest itself .
so , what do we actually want to manifest - a disabled person or a powerful person?
to call a human carelessly a mere child could be enough to restrict her and put her ideas to the test .
would not it be better to open your doors?
a person who opened doors for me was my pediatrician at a .i . dupont institute in wilmington in delaware .
his name is dr . pizzutillo . an italo-american whose name , as you can imagine , most americans could not pronounce correctly . so he was always dr . p . called  .
and dr . p . always wore very colorful flies and was just made to work with children .
i just loved the time i spent in this hospital - except for my physiotherapy .
i had to repeat some exercises seemingly endlessly with these thick , elastic bands - in different colors - that you know to build my leg muscles . and i hated those bands more than anything else . i hated her . i cursed her . i hated her .
and imagine , even as a five-year-old child with dr . p . negotiated and tried to stop these exercises , of course , without success .
and one day he observed me on one of my exercises - those exercises were just tiresome and merciless - and he said to me , &quot; wow , aimee , you are such a strong , powerful and young girl , you will one day become one rip these ribbons .
and if you can do it , i &apos;ll give you a hundred dollars .  &quot;
of course that was just a trick from dr . p . so i do the exercises i did not want to do , with the prospect of becoming the richest five-year-old girl in the infirmary on the second floor . but he actually got me to see my daily exercise horror with new eyes . this became a new and promising experience .
and today i wonder how much his vision of me as a strong and powerful young girl has shaped my self-image and how i could imagine myself as a naturally strong , powerful and atlethic man .
this is just one of many examples of how adults in power positions can stimulate a child &apos;s imagination .
but as the examples quoted in the synonym dictionaries prove , our language leaves us no room to imagine anything we would all wish for: enabling each one to see himself as a powerful human being .
our language lags behind the social changes that have in many cases been triggered by technological change .
from the medical point of view , of course , my legs , the laser surgery for vision impairments , knee joints made of titanium and artificial hip joints for aging bodies that allow people to really use all their possibilities and reach beyond the limits that they have their natural destiny has been to develop , not to speak of social networking platforms , to enable people to create their own identity , to define themselves in their own way , and thus to orient themselves around the world to groups they choose for themselves .
maybe this technological change will make it clearer than ever that there has always been another truth , namely , that every human being can give something very special and powerful to society and that human adaptability is our greatest plus .
the human capacity to adapt - that &apos;s an interesting story , because people keep asking me to tell them how to deal with adversity and i &apos;ll confess something to them: that sentence never suited me and i &apos;ve always felt very uncomfortable with it to answer the questions of people to do so , and i think , gradually , i understand why .
this notion of dealing with adversity lives from the idea that success or happiness depends on mastering a challenge without being influenced by the associated experience . as if my life had been so successful because i could avoid the possible fall of a life with a prosthesis , or whatever people perceive me to be with my disability .
but the truth is that we are changing . of course , we are shaped by the challenge , whether it is physical , emotional or even both
and i say that &apos;s good .
adversity is not an obstacle that we have to go around to better cope with our lives .
adversity is simply part of our life .
and i tend to see adversity as my shadow .
sometimes i realize that he is very present , sometimes he is barely visible , but he is always with me .
and i do not want to reduce the impact or the severity of a person &apos;s fight .
there are adversities and challenges in life , and they are all too real and each person is treated differently . but the question is not whether we get along with adversity or not , but how we handle it .
so , not only are we responsible for protecting the people we love from fatalities , but also preparing them to deal with them well .
and we do not please our children if we make them feel they can not adapt .
there are two things that must be clearly separated: firstly , the medical fact of being amputated and , secondly , the social opinion about whether i am disabled or not .
and to be honest , the only real and lasting disability i have had to deal with is that the world constantly thinks that it can describe me with these definitions .
in our desire to protect the people we care about and share with them the cold , harsh truth about their medical prognosis , or even to forecast the quality of life that awaits them , we need to be careful that we do not lay the groundwork for it that someone is actually disabled .
perhaps the current concept , which only looks at what is broken in one , and how we fix it , is more disabling for everyone than the pathology itself .
if we do not treat a person holistically , and do not perceive and acknowledge all of his or her powers and potential , we create another illness in addition to the natural struggle that they may have to wage .
we degrade a person who has value for our society .
so we need to look beyond patology and focus on all areas of human potential .
most importantly , there is a connection between the perception of our shortcomings and our great ingenuity .
we should not devalue or deny these challenging times , we should neither try to avoid them , nor should we sweep them under the carpet , but it is about identifying opportunities in the adversities .
i may be more concerned with clarifying that we do not necessarily have to overcome adversity , but that we are open to embrace them , to put them on their toes and even to use a fight expression and maybe even to dance with it ,
and maybe we manage to see adversities as something natural , coherent and useful , and thus feel less burdened by their presence .
this year we celebrate the 200th birthday of charles darwin and when he wrote about evolution 150 years ago , darwin pointed out , in my eyes , something very true in the human character .
i would put it this way: not the strongest of its kind survives , nor is it the most intelligent of its kind , but the one who can best adapt to changes .
conflicts create creativity .
not only from darwin &apos;s work do we know that man &apos;s ability to survive and flourish is determined by the struggle of the human mind to change through conflict .
so , again , change and adaptation are the greatest abilities of man .
and maybe we only know from what wood we are carved , when we are really tested .
perhaps this is precisely the sense of adversity , a sense of self , a sense of our own power .
we can give ourselves something like that .
we can give a new meaning to adversity that goes beyond hard times .
maybe we can see adversity as a change .
adversity represents a change we have not yet adapted to .
i think the biggest harm we &apos;ve done to ourselves is believing that we should be normal .
let &apos;s face it - who is normal?
there is no normal .
there is the usual . the typical . but not the normal one . and would you really want to meet this poor , gray person , if they really existed?
i do not think so  .
it would be great to be able to exchange this pattern of normality for another of the possibilities , or the strength to even make it a little more dangerous . so we can unleash the power of a great many children and invite them to bring their very special and valuable skills to society .
anthropologists have found out that we have always demanded of people from members of our society one thing: to be useful and to make a contribution .
there is evidence that neanderthals were already carrying older people and people with severe physical injuries 60,000 years ago , and maybe that was because the life experience in these people &apos;s survival struggle was valuable to society:
they did not consider these people as broken and useless; they were treated as something special and valuable .

the great geneticist in the 20th century , t .g . dobzhansky , who was also a communicator in the russian orthodox church , once wrote an essay entitled &quot; nothing in biology makes any sense except in the light of evolution . &quot;

this is another look at the tree of life .
in this picture , i put a bush as a center of branches in all directions , because when looking at the edges of the tree of life , all existing species at the tips of these branches have succeeded in evolutionary terms: they have survived; it has demonstrated fitness and strength to its surroundings .
the human part of this bush , far to the end , of course , is what we are most interested in .
we branch from a common ancestor to modern chimpanzees about six or eight million years ago .
in this period , there were maybe 20 or 25 different species of hominids .
some came and went .
we have been here for about 130,000 years .
it seems we are quite isolated from the other parts of the tree of life , but for the most part , the basic mechanisms of our cells are pretty much the same .
do you realize that we can benefit from this and requisition the mechanisms of a common bacterium to produce the protein of the human insulin that is used to treat diabetics?
this is not like human insulin; this is the same protein that is chemically indistinguishable from what comes out of the pancreas .
and while we &apos;re talking about bacteria , are you aware that each one of us in our bowel carries more bacteria than there are cells in the rest of our body?
maybe 10 times as many .
i mean , do you think , when antony damasio asks for her self-image , do you think about the bacteria?
our intestine is a wonderfully hospitable environment for these bacteria .
it is warm , it is dark , it is humid , it is very cozy .
and you will provide them with all the nutrients they could wish for without effort on their part .
it is really an easy way for bacteria , with occasional interruptions of the unintentional , ever forced bump to the exit .
but otherwise , they are a wonderful environment for these bacteria , just as they are important to their lives .
they help in the digestion of essential nutrients . and they protect you against certain diseases .
but what will come in the future?
are we in an evolutionary equilibrium in the form of a species?
or are we destined to become something else - something that may even be better adapted to the environment?
now let us take a step back to the time of the big bang 14 billion years ago - the earth , the solar system , about four and a half billion years ago - the first signs of life , maybe three to four billion years on earth - the first several cellular organisms , perhaps as much as 800 or a billion years ago - and then the human species , which eventually forms over the last 130,000 years .
in this vast unfinished symphony of the universe , life on earth is short-lived; the animal kingdom , like a single stroke; and human life , a little note ornament .
that was us .

well , when i was a freshman in college , i had my first biology classes .
i was fascinated by the elegance and beauty of biology .
i fell in love with the power of evolution , and i realized something very fundamental: in most of the existence of life in unicellular organisms , each cell splits , and all the genetic information of the cell is passed on in both daughter cells .
but by the time multicellular organisms appeared , things began to change .
sexual reproduction comes into the picture .
and most importantly , with the introduction of the sexual reproduction that passes the genome , the rest of the body becomes expendable .
in fact , one could say that the inevitable death of our body in evolution occurs at the same time as sexual reproduction .
now i have to admit , when i was a college student , i thought , okay , sex / death , sex / death , death for sex - it seemed pretty reasonable at the time , but with every passing year , my growing doubts have come ,
i came to understand the feelings of george burns , who performed his show in las vegas in the 90 &apos;s .
and one night , there &apos;s a knock on his hotel room door .
he opens the door  .
in front of him is a beautifully scantily dressed showgirl .
she looks at him and says , &quot; i &apos;m here for super sex . &quot;
 &quot; that &apos;s wonderful  &quot; says george , &quot; i &apos;ll take the soup . &quot;
i came to the realization as a doctor that i worked towards a goal that was different from the goal of evolution - not necessarily contradictory , just different .
i tried to save the body .
i wanted to keep ourselves healthy .
i wanted to restore my health after illness .
i wanted us to live a long and healthy life .
evolution is all about passing the genome on to the next generation , adapting and surviving generation after generation .
from an evolutionary point of view , you and i are developed like booster rockets to send the genetic cargo to the next level of the orbit and then drop into the sea .
i think we would all understand the mood that woody expressed to everyone when he said , &quot; i do not want to be immortal through my work .
i want to achieve it by not dying .  &quot;
evolution does not necessarily mean to prefer the longest life .
it does not necessarily privilege the biggest , the strongest or the fastest , and not even the smartest .
evolution privileges the creatures that best adapt to their environment .
it is only the test of survival and success
on the ocean floor there are bacteria that are thermophilic and thus survive the hot steam otherwise , if fish were there , it would result in vacuum cooked fish , yet they managed to make there a habitable environment for themselves .
so , what does that mean when we look back on what happens in evolution , and how we think about the place of humans in evolution , and most importantly how we look ahead to the next phase , i would say it &apos;s a series of possibilities there .
the first is that we will not evolve .
we have reached a kind of balance .
and the reasons for that would be that , first of all , through medicine , we managed to get a lot of genes that would otherwise have been eliminated and removed from the population .
and secondly , we as a special have configured our environment so that we have managed to adapt to it exactly as we adapt to it
and by the way , we walk and circulate and mingle so much that they can no longer say that isolation is necessary for evolution to take place .
a second possibility is that it will be an evolution of the traditional kind , of course , imposed by the forces of nature .
and the argument here is that the wheels of evolution are slowly grinding but they are relentless .
and as far as isolation goes , as we colonize distant planets as species , there will be isolation and the changes in the environment that evolution could naturally produce .
but there is a third possibility , a tantalizing , fascinating and frightening possibility
i call it neo-evolution - the new development that is not simply natural , but guided and decided by us as individuals . in the decisions we make .
well , how could that happen?
how could it be possible for us to do this?
first of all , let &apos;s look at the reality that people are making decisions about their descendants in some cultures today .
they are , in some cultures , the choice to have more men than women .
it is not necessarily good for the society , but it is what decides the individual and the family .
so think , if it were not only possible to choose the sex of your child but also to make genetic adjustments to the body that would cure and prevent illnesses .
what if you could make the genetic changes to eradicate diabetes or alzheimer &apos;s disease , or reduce your cancer risk or eliminate stroke?
would not you want to make these changes in your genes?
as we look ahead , these kinds of changes will be increasingly possible .
the human genome project began in 1990 and took 13 years to complete .
it cost $ 2.7 billion .
the following year , when it was finished in 2004 , they could do the same for $ 20 million in three to four months .
today you can complete a sequence of the three billion base pairs of the human genome at a cost of about $ 20,000 and in the space of about a week .
it will not be long before the reality is that there will be the $ 1,000 human genome , and it will be increasingly available to everyone .
a week ago , the national academy of engineering awarded the draper prize to francis arnold and willem stemmer , two scientists who independently developed techniques that encourage evolution &apos;s natural process to work faster and lead to desirable proteins in a more efficient manner - which frances arnold &quot; direct evolution &quot; is called  .
a few years ago , the lasker prize was awarded to scientist shinya yamanaka for his research in which he took an adult skin cell , a fibroblast , and by manipulating only four genes that induced cell to lead them to a pluripotent stem cell - one cell potentially capable of becoming any cell in your body .
these changes are coming .
the same technology that produces human insulin in bacteria can make viruses that can not only protect themselves , but can also serve to immunity to other viruses .
believe it or not , it is an experimental study in progress with vaccine against influenza grown in the cells of a tobacco plant .
can you imagine something good coming from tobacco?
this is all reality today and much more will be possible in the future .
then imagine only two other minor changes .
you can change the cells in your body , but what if you could change the cells of your descendants?
what if you could change the sperm and egg cells or the freshly fertilized egg to give your children a better chance of a healthier life - eliminating diabetes , eliminating hemophilia , reducing the risk of cancer?
who does not want healthier children?
and then the analytic technique , the same motor of science that can enable the changes to prevent disease , could also enable us to adopt adopt super-attributes , hyper-capacities - the better memory .
why have not you had the wit of a ken jenning , especially if you can expand it with the next generation of the watson machine?
why not have the fast muscle fibers that will enable them to run faster and longer?
why not live longer?
these will be irresistible .
and when we are in a position where we can pass it on to the next generation , and we can adopt the attributes we want , we will be transformed from old-style evolution to neo-evolution .
we will have a process that may typically take 100,000 years , and we can compress it to a thousand years - and maybe even the next 100 years .
these are the decisions that your grandchildren , or their grandchildren , must deal with .
do we use these decisions to shape a society that is better , more successful , friendlier?
or will we selectively choose different attributes that we want for some of us and not for others?
will we form a society that is more boring and uniformed , or more robust and versatile?
these are the kinds of questions we have to face .
and at the deepest of all , will we ever be able to develop the wisdom and master the wisdom that we will need to make these decisions wisely?
good and bad , and sooner than you might think these decisions will be up to us .
thank you  .
