i have just come back from a community that knows the secret of the continuation of humanity .
a place where women lead the scepter with sex  &apos;; hello  &apos;; say , and game determines the day - where fun is a serious matter .
and no , it &apos;s not burning man or san francisco .
my ladies and gentlemen , their relatives .
this is the world of wild bonobos in the jungle of kongo .
bonobos , together with chimpanzees , are our closest living relatives .
that means we have a common ancestor , an evolutionary grandmother who lived about six million years ago .
well , chimpanzees are known for their aggressiveness .
but , unfortunately , we have over-emphasized this aspect in the narrative of human evolution .
but bonobos show us the other side of the medal
while chimpanzees are led by big , scary people , the bonobo society is run by empowered females .
they have really come up with something , because that leads to a very tolerant society in which deadly violence has not been observed so far .
but unfortunately bonobos are least known among the primates .
they live in the depths of the congolese jungle , and are difficult to access .
the congo is a paradox - a land of extraordinary biodiversity and beauty , but also the heart of darkness itself - the scene of a violent conflict that has been raging for decades and has claimed almost as many lives as the first world war .
it is not surprising that this destruction also endangers the bonobos &apos; survival .
meat trading and the decline of tree population means that you can not even fill a stadium with the remaining bonobos - and even then we are not sure , to be honest .
and yet , in this land of violence and chaos , one can hear a hidden laugh that shakes the trees .
who are these relatives?
we know it as &quot; love instead of war &quot; monkeys , because they have frequent , even bisexual sex , with changing partners , and so settle conflicts and social issues .
i am not saying that is the answer to all humanitarian problems - because the life of the bonobos consists of more than the kama sutra .
bonobos - like humans - love to play their lives for a long time .
game does not just mean playing with children .
for us and her , playing is fundamental to building relationships and maintaining tolerance .
we learn confidence and the rules of the game .
play increases creativity and resilience , and it is above all about the creation of diversity - variety of interactions , diversity of behavior , variety of connections .
and when you watch bonobos playing , you see the evolutionary origins of human laughter , dance , and rituals .
play is the putty that keeps us together .
well , i do not know how they play , but i want to show you some unique shots straight out of the wilds .
first a ball game on bonobo-style - and i do not think about football .
so here we see a young female and male in a chase game .
look at what she does .
that could be the evolutionary origin of: &quot; she grabs him by the eggs . &quot;
only that i think he likes it more , does not it?
yes !
sex games are so common , both bonobos and humans .
and this video is really interesting , because it shows - and this video is really interesting , because it shows the wealth of ideas , with the new elements in the game brings - like the testicles - and also , as the game requires both trust and also promotes - while at the same time makes tremendous fun .
but the game is a shapeshifter .
play is a shapeshifter and it can take on many forms , some of which are a little quieter , imaginative , curious - perhaps to rediscover astonishment .
and i want to show you this is fuku , a young female , and she plays peacefully with water .
i think , like us , we sometimes play alone , and we explore the boundaries of our inner and outer worlds .
and it is this playful curiosity that lets us explore and interact . and the unexpected links that we form are the real breeding ground of creativity .
this is only a small taste of the insights that bonobos give us in our past and present .
but they also keep a secret for our future , a future in which we need to adapt to increasing challenges , greater creativity and greater cooperation .
the secret is that the game is the key to these skills .
in other words , play is our adaptation joker .
in order to successfully adapt to an unstable world , we have to play .
but will we make the most of our play-instinct?
game is not silly,
but essential .
for bonobos and humans alike , life does not consist of teeth and stealing alone .
when it seems hardly appropriate , game is perhaps the most necessary .
and therefore , my primate mates , let us embrace and play together this gift of evolution as we rediscover creativity , camaraderie , and amazement .
thank you  .
i would like you to introduce yourself two couples , in the middle of 1979 , on the very same day , at the exact same moment , each bears a baby - okay .
so two couples , each testifies a baby .
now i do not want you to spend too much time imagining the generation , because if you imagine the generation all the time , you will not listen to me .
imagine that only for a moment .
and in this scenario , imagine that in one case the sperm carries a y-chromosome that hits the x chromosome of the egg .
and in the other case , the sperm carries an x ​​chromosome that hits the x chromosome of the egg .
both are capable of survival , both ignite .
we will come back to these people later .
i have two hats on most of my activities .
under a hat , i deal with the history of the anatomy .
i am a graduate in history , and in this case i study how people deal with anatomy - i . human bodies , animal bodies - how they dealt with bodily fluids , with body concepts; as they thought about bodies .
the other hat i have worn in my work is the activist , as a patient advocate - or , as i sometimes say , an impatient solicitor - of people who are patients of doctors .
in this case , i have worked with people who have body types that question social norms .
for example , i worked with people who are siamese twins , two people in one body .
i worked with people with dwarfism - people who were smaller than usual .
and very often i have worked with intersex people whose sex is atypical - that is , people who do not have the average male or female body type .
and as a general term we can use the word intersexuality .
intersexuality occurs in many different forms .
i &apos;ll give you just a few examples of the possible types of gender variants that do not conform to the male or female standard .
in one case you may have someone with an xy chromosome base , and the syst gen of the y chomosome makes the protogonads , which we all have fetal stage , grow into testicles .
and that is why the testes release testosterone at the fetal stage .
but because this individual lacks the receptors to detect testosterone , the body does not respond to the testosterone .
and that &apos;s called an androgen receptor defect .
so much testosterone , but no reaction to it .
as a consequence , the body evolves more in line with the typical female pattern .
when the child is born , it looks like a girl .
she is a girl . she is educated as a girl .
but it usually lasts until she reaches puberty and her breasts grow and develop , but she does not get her period until someone comes to the conclusion that there is something different here .
and they do a few tests and find out that instead of eggs and uterus , they actually carry testicles and that they have a chromosome .
it is important to understand that this person might actually be male , but they are not really .
women , like men , have something in our bodies called adrenaline glands .
they are in the back part of our body .
and the adrenals produce androgens , which are virilizing hormones .
most women , like me - i think being a typical woman - i do not know my actual chromosome construction , but i think i probably have the most typical - most women like me actually respond to androgenic .
we produce androgen and we respond to androgens .
the result is that someone like me actually has a brain that was more androgenic than a woman who was born with testicles and has an androgen receptor defect .
sex is really complicated; intersexuals are not only in the middle of the gender spectrum - in a sense , they can be spread across the entire bandwidth,
another example: a few years ago , i received a call from a 19-year-old man who was born a boy , raised a boy , had a girlfriend , had sex with his girlfriend , lived as a man , and he had just found out that he was had ovaries and a uterus .
he had an extreme form of a congenital adrenocortical hyperbole .
he has xx chromosomes , and in the womb , his adrenals were so active that they essentially created a male hormone environment .
and as a consequence , his genitals became masculine , his brain exposed to the more typical male part of the hormonal spectrum .
and when he was born he looked like a boy - no one suspected anything .
and only when he turned 19 did he get enough medical problems , in fact because he internally menstruated , that the doctors found out that he was female inside .
okay , just a quick example of a variant of intersex .
some people with xx chromosomes develop something called ovotestis , where ovarian tissue is encased in testicular tissue .
we do not know exactly why that happens .
so sex can occur in many different ways .
the reason why children with this type of body - be it adult or siamese twins or intersex - is often adapted to the standard by surgeons is not because it would be beneficial to their physical health .
in many cases , these people are perfectly healthy .
the reason why they often undergo various types of surgical intervention is because they threaten our social categories .
our system is typically based on the idea that a particular anatomical form is associated with a particular identity .
so we have the concept of being a woman means having a female identity; being a black person is said to have an african anatomy in view of your own story .
so we have this terribly simplistic idea .
and when we are confronted with a body that really is something very different for us , it confuses us in terms of those categorizations .
so we have a lot of romantic ideas about individualism in our culture .
and our nation is really based on a very romantic concept of individualism .
now you can imagine how startling it is when children are born who are two persons in one body,
where lately i had the most excitement was the south african runner caster semenya last year whose gender was questioned at the leichatlethik world championship in berlin .
many journalists called me with the question: &quot; what test will you do that tells us whether caster semenya is female or male? &quot;
and i had to explain to journalists that this test does not exist .
we now know that sex is complicated enough , that we have to admit that for us nature does not draw a line between male and female , or between male and intersex and female and intersex; in the main we are the ones who pretend that dividing line as natural .
so we have such a situation where , as our science progresses , we have to acknowledge all the more that these categories , which we have considered to be stable anatomical categories , which produced very simple associations to create lasting identity categories , are much blurred as we assumed .
and that &apos;s not just about sex .
it also applies to race , which turns out to be much more complicated than our terminology has allowed .
in our considerations , we get into all sorts of uncomfortable areas .
for example , we come across the fact that we share at least 95 percent of our dna with chimpanzees .
what should we do with the fact that we differ from them only by a few nucleotides?
as we continue to advance our science , we are increasingly entering a zone of discomfort where we must acknowledge that the simplistic categories we had were all too simplistic .
we see this in all possible areas of human life .
one of the areas in which we see this , for example , in our culture today , in today &apos;s united states , are the struggles over the beginning and the end of life .
we have difficult conversations about the point at which a body becomes human , so that it has another right than the fetal life .
nowadays , we have very difficult arguments - perhaps not as public as in medicine - about the question of when someone is dead .
our ancestors never used to struggle with the question of when someone was dead .
at most , they held someone under their noses , and if they moved , they have not yet buried them .
if she stopped moving , she was buried .
but today we find ourselves in a situation where we want to remove people &apos;s vital organs and transplant them to other people .
and as a result we get caught up in the fight with the really tough question of when someone is dead . and that puts us in a really difficult position where we do not have as simple categories as we used to .
now you might think that the whole breakdown of categories would really make someone like me happy .
i am politically progressive , i defend people with unusual bodies , but i have to admit , it makes me nervous .
to realize that these categories are really much more insecure than we thought makes me tense .
and it makes me tense with regard to considerations concerning democracy .
so to tell you about this tension , i have to confess first that i &apos;m a big fan of founding fathers .
i know they were racist , i know they were sexists , but they were great .
i think they were so brave and strong and so radical in what they did that every couple of years i catch sight of the lumpy musical 1776 , and it &apos;s not because of the music that &apos;s absolutely forgotten .
it is because of what happened in 1776 with the founding fathers .
in my opinion , the founding fathers were the original anatomical activists , and that is why .
what they rejected was an anatomical concept , and they replaced it with another that was radical and beautiful , and that was relevant to us for 200 years .
as you all remember , our founding fathers rejected the concept of monarchy . and the monarchy was based on a very simplified concept of anatomy .
the rulers of the old world had no concept based on the dna , but they had a concept of the right of birth .
they had a blue blood concept .
they were of the opinion that people who had political power should have this political power because of the inherited bloodline from grandfather to father to son and so on .
the founding fathers rejected this idea and replaced it with a new anatomical concept , and that concept said that all men were created equal .
they leveled the playing field and decided that , anatomically speaking , the similarities did not count the differences . and that was very radical .
now they did that because they were part of an enlightenment system in which two things grew together .
democracy was growing , but at the same time science was also growing .
if you look at the founding fathers &apos; story , it becomes clear that many of them were very interested in science , and they were interested in a concept of a naturalistic world .
they went away from explanations of a supernatural nature , rejecting things like a supernatural concept of power , where the transfer is based on a very vague concept of birth right .
they moved towards a naturalistic concept .
and when you look at the declaration of independence , for example , you talk about nature and the god of nature .
they do not talk about god and the nature of god .
they talk about the power of nature to tell us who we are .
and as part of that they gave us a concept of anatomical similarities .
and with that , they have wonderfully prepared the future civil rights movement .
they did not think so , but what they did for us was great .
so what happened next year?
for example , women who sought the right to vote , the concept of founding fathers , who says anatomical togetherness is more important than the anatomical difference , and said , &quot; that we have a uterus and ovaries is not significant enough as a difference that we do not should have the right to the right to full citizenship , to own the right property , etc , etc .  &quot;
and women argued that successfully .
next came the successful civil rights movement , where we saw people like sojourner truth , who spoke about it ,  &quot; am i not a woman? &quot;
we find men marching in the ranks of the civil rights movement who say , &quot; i am a man . &quot;
again , people of different skin color who rely on anatomical similarities before anatomical differences , again , succeed .
we see the same in the disability rights movement .
of course , the problem is that as we begin to look at all the similarities , we must begin to question why we maintain certain separations .
now , mind you , i want to maintain certain divisions , anatomically speaking , in our culture .
for example , i do not want to give a fish the same rights as a human being .
i do not want to say that we should completely distance ourselves from the anatomy .
i do not want to say that 5 year olds should have the right to give their consent to sex or to marry .
so there are some anatomical divisions that make sense to me , and which i think we should keep .
but the challenge is to find out who they are and why we keep them and if they make sense .
so let &apos;s get back to those two beings who were conceived at the beginning of the lecture .
we have two beings , both in the middle of 1979 , begotten in exactly the same day .
imagine that one of them , maria , was born prematurely three months ago , so she was born on 1 june 1980 .
heinrich , on the other hand , is born on the birth date , so he was born on 1 march 1980,
just because of the fact that maria was born three months early , she gains all possible rights three months before heinrich - to have the right consensual sex , the right to choose , the right to drink .
heinrich has to wait for all this , not because he actually has another biological age , only because of the time of his birth .
we find other oddities regarding what your rights are .
heinrich , assuming he is male - although i did not tell you he &apos;s the one with xy - assuming he &apos;s male , he can now be confiscated , something maria does not have to worry about .
maria , on the other hand , can not exercise the same rights in all federal states that heinrich has in all states , and indeed the right to marry .
heinrich can marry a woman in every state , but today maria can only marry a woman in a few states .
so we have these permanent anatomical categories that are in many respects problematic and questionable .
and for me , the question arises: what will we do as our science makes such progress in anatomy that we reach a point where we have to admit that a democracy based on anatomy could fall apart?
i do not want to give up science , but at the same time it sometimes feels like science is becoming self-reliant .
where are we going?
it seems that our culture takes on a kind of pragmatic attitude: &quot; well , we have to draw a line somewhere , so we draw it somewhere &quot;
but a lot of people get caught in a very strange position .
so , for example , at one point texas decided that marrying a man means you do not have a y chromosome , and marrying a woman means you have a y chromosome .
now , in practice , people are not being tested for their chromosomes .
but it is also very bizarre , because of the story i first told them about the androgen receptor defect .
if we look at one of the founding fathers of modern democracy , dr . martin luther king , he offers us in his &quot; i have a dream &quot; a kind of solution .
he says that we should not treat people according to &quot; their skin color , but according to the property of their character &quot; judge , and so goes beyond anatomy .
and i want to say , &quot; yes , that sounds like a really good idea . &quot;
but how do you do that in practice?
how do you judge people based on their characteristics?
i would also like to point out that i am not sure that we should distribute human rights in such a way because i have to admit that i know a number of golden retrievers who are likely to earn social benefits rather than some i know .
i &apos;d like to say that i probably also know some bright labrador dogs who are more capable of making more informed , smarter , and mature decisions about their sexual relationships than some 40-year-olds i know .
so how should we operationalize the question about the properties of the character?
it turns out to be really difficult .
and a part of me wonders what if the content of a personality was something that would be machine-readable in the future - could be visualized using fmri?
do we really want to go in this direction?
i &apos;m not sure where we &apos;re going .
what i know is that it really seems important to think about the idea that the usa is the leader in thinking about issues of democracy .
in our quest for democracy , we have done our job really well , and i think we would do well in the future .
we do not have the situation , as is the case in iran , for example , where a man sexually attracted to other men is punished with death unless he is ready to undergo gender reassignment , in which case he may stay alive .
conditions of this kind do not exist with us .
i am glad to say that we do not have these conditions - a surgeon whom i spoke with a few years ago , who had brought along a pair of siamese twins to separate them , in part to make a name for themselves .
but when i phoned him and asked him why he made these interventions - they were high-risk operations - his response was that in his country , those kids would be treated very badly , and so he had to do it .
i answered him , &quot; now you have considered political asylum rather than an operation to separate it? &quot;
the united states offer incredible opportunities to allow people to be who they are without having to change for the sake of the state .
that &apos;s why i think we have to be leaders .
well , just to finish , i &apos;d like to say that i talked a lot about the fathers .
and i would like to think about the possibilities democracy might look like , or might have looked like , if we had more mothers involved .
and i want to say something that is a bit radical to a feminist , and i think that there could be different types of insights that come from different types of anatomy , especially when people use group thinking .
for years , because i was interested in intersexuality , i was also interested in research on gender differences .
and one of the things that i found really interesting is the difference between men and women in terms of how they think and act in the world .
what we have learned from cross-cultural studies is that women , on average - not every , but on average - are more likely to pay close attention to complex social relationships and caring for people who are vulnerable within the group .
and therefore , when we think about it we have an interesting situation ahead of us .
years ago , when i was doing postgraduate studies , one of my supervisors who knew my interest in feminism asked me - i consider myself a feminist , so it still does - a really peculiar question .
he said , &quot; tell me what feminine feminine is . &quot;
and i thought , &quot; well , that &apos;s the stupidest question i &apos;ve ever heard .
feminism is about eliminating clichés about gender , so feminism is not feminine .
but the more i thought about his question , the more i thought that there could be something feminine in feminism .
that is to say , there could be something , on average , that distinguishes female brains from male ones , which makes us more attentive to highly complex social relationships , and more attentive to caring for the vulnerable .
so , if the founding fathers were extremely wary of figuring out how individuals could be protected from the state , it &apos;s possible that if we brought more mothers into that concept , we &apos;d get more conceptually , not just how to protect , but how we could protect each other can provide .
and maybe that &apos;s where we have to go in the future , if we &apos;re going to bring democracy beyond anatomy - less thinking about the individual body in terms of identity , and thinking more about relationships .
so if we try to make a more perfect connection as a people , we think about what we can do for each other .

if you take this picture now - i am originally italian , and every boy in italy grows up in his room with this picture . but the reason i am showing them this is that something very interesting happened in formula 1 over the past decades .
some time ago , when they wanted to win a formula 1 race , they took their budget and put their money on a good driver and a good car .
and if the car and the driver were good enough , then they won the race .
nowadays , when you want to win a race , you really need something like this - something that the car monitors in real time , has a few thousand sensors , transmits the information from the car , passes that information on to the system and then processes it they then use this to send back decisions to this car and change things in real time while information is collected .
in engineering teams , you would call this a real-time control system .
and basically , it &apos;s a system that consists of two parts - a sensing part and a reacting part .
what is interesting these days is the real-time control systems starting to emerge in our lives .
our cities have just been equipped with networks and electronics in the last few years .

as an artist , the connection is very important to me .
through my work i try to express that people are not separated from nature and that everything is connected .
the first time i was in the antarctic almost 10 years ago , where i saw my first icebergs .
i felt awe .
my heart was racing , i was dizzy , trying to grasp what was in front of me .
the icebergs around me stood almost 60 meters out of the water . and i could only marvel that this was a snowflake on another snowflake , year after year .
icebergs are born as calves of a glacier or by breaking out of ice floes .
every iceberg has its own individual personality .
they interact clearly with their environment and their experiences .
some refuse to give up and cling to the bitter end , while others can not stand it anymore and collapse in a fit of dramatic passion .
when looking at an iceberg , it is easy to think that they are isolated , separate and alone , much as we humans often see each other .
but the truth is far from it .
as an iceberg melts , i breathe in its prehistoric atmosphere .
while the ice melts , it releases mineral-rich drinking water that nourishes many forms of life .
i take my pictures of these icebergs as if i were portraits of my ancestors , knowing that in these individual moments they exist in that wise , and will never again exist like that .
it is not a death when they melt; it is not an end , but a continuation of your path along the life cycle .
part of the ice in the icebergs i photograph is very young - a few thousand years old .
and part of the ice is more than 100,000 years old .
the last pictures i would like to show you , show an iceberg , which i photographed in kekertsuatsiak in greenland .
very seldom does one actually witness a rolling iceberg .
here you can see it now .
on the left side you will see a small boat .
that &apos;s about five meters long .
please pay attention to the shape of the iceberg and where it is located on the waterline .
you can see here , he starts to roll , and the boat has moved to the other side , the man is standing there .
that &apos;s the average size of a greenland iceberg .
it protrudes about 120 feet out of the water , or 40 meters .
and this video is in real time .
and just like that , the iceberg shows you another side of his personality .

the idea behind the stuxnet computer worm is pretty simple in itself .
we do not want iran to build the bomb .
the most important item there for the development of nuclear weapons is the uranium enrichment facility in natanz .
the gray boxes you see here are real-time control systems .
if we succeed in compromising the systems , controlling the speeds and valves , then we can actually cause a lot of problems with the centrifuge .
these gray boxes do not work with windows software; they are based on a completely different technology .
but if we manage to have an effective windows virus on a laptop used by a maintenance engineer to configure this gray box , then we &apos;re in business .
and that &apos;s the planning behind stuxnet .
so we start with a windows-dropper .
the user data are transferred to the gray box , damage the centrifuge and the iranian nuclear energy program delays - order executed .
this is a child game , right?
i want to tell you how we found that out .
when we started our research on stuxnet six months ago , it was completely unknown what the purpose of this construct was .
the only thing that we knew is very , very complicated in terms of the windows part , the dropper part , it made use of countless zero day weak spots .
and there seemed to be something to do with those gray boxes , these real-time control systems .
this caught our attention and we started a lab project in which we infected our area with stuxnet and examined this construct .
and then something funny happened .
stuxnet behaved like a lab rat who did not like our cheese - sniffed it , but did not want to eat it .
that made no sense in my eyes .
and after experimenting with different types of cheese , i realized that this is a targeted attack .
completely focused on a specific goal .
the dropper actively roams the gray box when a special configuration is detected . even if the special program that is trying to infect it is running on this target .
and if not , then stuxnet does nothing .
well , that really caught my interest and we started working around it , almost around the clock , because i thought well , we do not know what the goal is .
it could be , say , a us- american power plant , or a chemical plant in germany .
so we should soon find out what the goal is .
so we extracted and decompiled the attack code and found that it is structured into two digital warheads - one smaller and one bigger .
and we also noticed that they were very professionally designed by people who obviously had all insider information .
they knew all the bits and bytes they needed to attack .
you probably even knew the size of the machine operator &apos;s shoe .
they knew everything .
and if you &apos;ve already noticed that the stuxnet dropper is complex and high-tech , let me tell you: the payload is a science in itself .
it &apos;s much more demanding than anything we &apos;ve ever seen .
here you can see a part of this attack code .
we &apos;re talking about - about 15,000 lines of code .
looks pretty much like old-fashioned assembler language .
and i want to tell you how we were able to get out of this code .
so what we were looking for first was system function calls because we know what they are .
and then we searched for timers and data structures and tried to connect them with the real world - with potential goals in the real world .
so we needed theories about those goals that we could prove or disprove .
in order to establish these theories , we recall that it is definitely high-level sabotage , it must be a high-quality target and most likely it is in iran because most of the infections were reported there .
well , in this area there are not several thousand goals .
basically , it goes out to the buschehr nuclear power plant and the uranium enrichment plant in natanz .
so i said to my assistant , &quot; bring me a list of all experts for centrifuges and power plants from our customer base . &quot;
and i called her and tapped her knowledge to compare her expertise with what we had found out in the code and in the data .
and that worked very well .
so , it was possible for us to connect the small digital warhead with the rotor control .
the rotor is this moving part inside the centrifuge , this black object you see .
and if you manipulate the speed of this rotor , then you are actually able to crack the rotor and finally even make the centrifuge explode .
what we also found out is that the goal of the attack was actually to make it slow and sinister - obviously in an attempt to make maintenance engineers despair , so they would not be able to figure it out quickly .
the big digital warhead - we got an insight by looking very closely at data and data structures .
so , for example , the number 164 stands out very much from this code; you can not miss it .
i began to work through scientific literature on how these centrifuges are built in natance and found out that they are structured in so-called cascades , and each cascade contains 164 centrifuges .
so that made sense , it matched .
and it got even better .
these centrifuges in iran are divided into 15 so-called levels .
and guess what we found in the attack code?
an almost identical structure .
so again , that fit really well together .
and this gave us great confidence for what we studied here .
well , do not get me wrong , it just did not work that way .
these results were achieved through several weeks of hard work .
and often we just ran into a dead end and had to regroup .
in any case , we found out that both digital warheads were actually heading for the same goal , but from different directions .
the little warhead takes over a cascade and over-rotates and slows down the rotors , and the big warhead interacts with six cascades to manipulate valves .
so , by and large , we are very confident that we have actually determined what the goal is .
it is natural and it is only natural .
so we do not have to worry about other destinations being hit by stuxnet .
here is something really cool stuff that we discovered - really knocked me off my feet .
down there is the gray box , and on top of it you can see the centrifuge .
well , what causes this thing is that it intercepts the incoming values ​​of sensors - such as pressure sensors and vibration sensors - and it provides legitimate code that still runs during the attack , with inaccurate incoming data .
and indeed , this wrong inbound data from stuxnet has already been recorded .
so it &apos;s the same as in hollywood movies , where during the chase the surveillance camera is fed with previously recorded video footage .
that &apos;s cool , right?
the idea here is obviously not just outsmarting the machine operators in the control room .
in fact it is much more dangerous and aggressive .
the idea is to outsmart a digital security system .
we need digital security systems where a human operator can not react fast enough .
in a power plant , for example , when the large steam turbine gets too fast , relief valves must be opened within milliseconds .
of course , this can not be done by a human machine operator .
so at this point we need digital security systems .
and when they are endangered , really bad things can happen .
the power plant can explode .
and neither the machine operator , nor the security system will notice .
that &apos;s scary .
but it gets worse .
and what i am saying now is very important .
think about it  . this attack is generic .
he has nothing specifically to do with centrifuges , with uranium enrichment .
so , for example , it would work just as well - in a power plant or in an automobile factory .
it is generic .
and you do not have to - as the attacker - you do not have to attach the usability data via a usb-stick , as we have seen with stuxnet .
you could also use ordinary worm technology to spread .
you just have to spread it as much as possible .
and when you do that , you finally get a cyber mass destruction weapon .
that &apos;s the consequence we have to face .
so , unfortunately , the largest number of targets for such attacks is not in the middle east .
they are located in the united states , in europe and japan .
so all these green areas , these are the most targeted environments .
we have to face the consequences and we better prepare ourselves .
thank you  .
i have a question  .
ralph , it was reported a lot that people suspect that the mossad is the main organization behind it .
is that your opinion?
okay , you really want to know that?
yes  . okay .
in my opinion , the mossad is involved , but the driving force is not israel .
so the driving force behind it is cyber supermacht .
there is only one and these are the united states - fortunately , fortunately .
otherwise our problem would be even bigger .
thank you for scared the hell out of us . thanks ralph .

this is a representation of your brain . her brain can be divided in half .
this is the left half , which is the logical side and then the right half , which is the intuitive side .
if we had a scale to measure the aptitude of each hemisphere , we could represent its brain .
for example , this would be someone who is completely logical .
this would be someone who is completely intuitive .
now , where would you position your brain on this scale?
some of us may have opted for one of these extreme ones , but i think that for most people in the audience , their brain looks something like this - with a high aptitute that is in both hemispheres at the same time .
it &apos;s not as if they &apos;re both exclusive or the like .
they can be logical and intuitive .
and so i see myself as one of the people , along with most other experimental quantum physicists who need a good deal of logic to put the complex ideas together .
but at the same time we need a good amount of intuition to actually do the experiments .
how do we develop this intuition? well , we love to play with things .
so we go out and play with it and then look how it reacts . and then we begin to develop our intuition .
basically , they do the same thing .
so one of the intuitions that you have developed over the years is probably that a thing can only be in one place at the same time .
i mean , it may sound weird to think that a thing is in two different places at the same time , but you were not born with that idea , you developed it .
i remember seeing a kid playing on a floor level .
he was still a toddler and not very good at it and he was constantly falling .
but i bet that playing with the floor threshold taught him a very valuable lesson , namely that big things do not just let you pass and that they stay in one place .
and that is a great conceptual model of the world , as long as you are not a particle physicist .
it would be a terrible model for a particle physicist , because they do not play with sleepers , but with those little weird particles .
and when they play with their particles , they spot all sorts of strange things - like flying right through walls , or at the same time being in two different places .
and so they all wrote down these observations , calling them the theory of quantum mechanics .
that was the state of physics a few years ago; you needed quantum mechanics to describe the tiny , tiny particles .
but you did not need them to describe the big everyday objects around us .
that did not like my intuition and that maybe only because i do not play with so many parts .
well , sometimes i play with them , but not very often .
and i have never seen her
i mean , no one has ever seen a particle .
but my logical side did not like it either .
because if all things consist of particles and all these particles follow the rules of quantum mechanics , should not everything just follow the rules of quantum mechanics?
i see no reason why that should not be so .
so i would feel much better with the whole thing , if we could somehow show that everyday objects also follow the rules of quantum mechanics .
so , a few years ago , i set about doing just that .
i created one .
this is the first object you can see that was in the quantum mechanical superposition .
so what we are looking at here is a tiny computer chip .
and you can see this green dot in the middle there .
this is the piece of metal that i will talk about in a moment .
this is a picture of the object .
here i enlarge it a bit . we are looking right here in the center .
and here is a really huge close-up of this little piece of metal .
so what we &apos;re looking at is a small piece of metal shaped like a springboard and overhanging the edge .
so i made this thing in a similar way to making a computer chip .
i went into a clean room with new silicon wafers , and racked for 100 hours on all the big appliances .
for the last bit i had to build my own machine - to get this hole shaped like a swimming pool and the thing .
this thing has the ability to be in the quantum mechanical superposition but it needs a little help to do it .
look , let me give you an analogy .
you know how uncomfortable it is to be in a full elevator?
i mean , when i &apos;m alone in the elevator , i do all sorts of strange things but then other people get in and i stop doing all those things because i do not want to bother them or , frankly , bother them .
so quantum mechanics says that immovable objects feel the same way .
the riders for immovable objects are not just humans , but also the light that shines on them and the wind that blows past them and the heat of space .
and so we knew that if we wanted to see that this piece of metal behaves quantum mechanically , we had to throw all the passengers out .
and we did that .
we switched off the light , put it in a vacuum and sucked all the air out and cooled it almost to the now , all alone in the elevator , the piece of metal was free to behave as it would .
so we measured his movement .
we realized that it is really moving strangely .
instead of just lying there , it vibrated . the way it vibrated was like this , like an expanding bellows pulling together .
by giving it a little push , we could cause it to vibrate and not vibrate at the same time - something only allowed in quantum mechanics .
what i am about to tell you is something truly fantastic .
what does it mean for an object to vibrate at the same time and not to vibrate?
let us think about the atoms .
so the first case: all the trillions of atoms that make up the piece of metal lie still and at the same time the same atoms move up and down .
only at precise times do they agree .
the rest of the time they are delocalised .
that is , each atom is at the same time in two different places , which means that the whole piece of metal is in two different places .
i think that &apos;s really cool .
for real  .
it was worth it to include me in the clean room for all these years . because , check that , the difference in scale between a single atom and this piece of metal is about the same as the difference between the piece of metal and them .
so if a single atom can be in two different places at the same time , this piece of metal can be in two different places , then why not them too?
i mean , that &apos;s just what my logical side says .
so , imagine that you are in several places at the same time , how would that feel?
how would you be aware that your body is delocalized in space?
there is another aspect of the story .
when we warm it up , turned on the light and looked in the box , we saw that the piece of metal was always in one piece .
and so i had to develop this new intuition that it looks like all the objects in the elevator are in reality only quantum objects that have come together in a small room .
you hear a lot of talk about how quantum mechanics says that everything is connected to everything .
well , that &apos;s not quite right;
there is more behind it , it goes even deeper .
it &apos;s like these connections , their connections with all the things around them , literally define who they are . and that is the profound and strange of quantum mechanics .
many thanks  .
in 2007 , i decided that we should reconceptualize how we think about economic development .
it should be our goal that , when families think about where they want to live and work , they have the opportunity to choose between at least one handful of different cities , all of which are in competition for new residents .
at the moment we are far away from this goal .
there are billions of people in developing countries who do not even have a single city to welcome .
but the amazing thing about cities is that they are so much more valuable than they cost to build .
so we could easily equip the world with dozens , maybe even hundreds of new cities .
this may sound preposterous for you , as long as you &apos;ve never thought about new cities .
just swap the construction of flats against the ones of cities .
imagine , half the people who want to live in flats already do that; the other half but not yet .
you could try to increase the capacity of already existing flats .
but do you know what you would get involved with , in these apartments and their surroundings laws prevail to avoid complaints and distract from expansion .
it turns out to be very difficult to comply with all these extensions .
but you could go to a completely new place , build a completely new block of flats , provided the laws there would support such construction and not hinder him .
so i suggested that governments create new space that would provide enough space for a city and gave them a name: charter cities .
later i found out that , around the same time , javier and octavio were thinking about the challenge of a reform in honduras .
they knew that every year about 75,000 hondurans left their country to emigrate to the united states , and they wanted to know what they could do to make sure that these people could stay in their country and do the same in honduras can  .
at this point , javier said to octavio , &quot; what happens if we take an unpopulated area of ​​our country - which if we just give it to a message - part of the american embassy , ​​part of the canadian embassy - and then people working under canadian or united states rules , can you work there , and can you do anything on the surface of these messages that you would normally need to go to canada or america?  &quot;
in the summer of 2009 , honduras suffered from a violent constitutional crisis .
at the next scheduled elections , pepe lobo won in a turnaround whose program promised both renewal and conciliation .
he asked octavio to become his chief of staff .
in the meantime , i was preparing for a lecture at tedglobal .
by improving , trying out and examining consumers , i have tried to reduce this complicated concept of charter city to the essential elements .
the first point is the importance of laws , laws that determine that it is not allowed to disturb already existing apartment owners .
we pay great attention to new technologies , but progress requires technology and legislation . and it &apos;s usually laws that hold us back .

when i was a graduate student at stanford university , i went to this very very exclusive grocery store; at least then he was really exclusive .
it was a shop , the draeger  &apos;; his name was .
this shop , it was almost like a fun park .
they had 250 different types of mustard and vinegar and over 500 different types of fruit and vegetables and more than two dozen different types of water - and that was at a time when we were actually drinking tap water .
i loved going to this business; but at one point i wondered , well , how come you never buy anything?
here is the olive oil gang .
they had over 75 different varieties of olive oil , including those that were trapped in boxes that came from thousand-year-old olive trees .
so one day i decided to visit the store manager , and i asked the manager , &quot; does this model really work to provide people with all these options? &quot;
and he pointed to the busloads of tourists who came every day and usually had their cameras with them .
we decided to do an experiment and we chose jam for our experiment .
here is her jam shop .
they had 348 different types of jam .
we set up a small tasting corner right at the entrance to the store .
there were six different tastes of jam or 24 different tastes of jam , and we looked at two things: firstly , in which case would more people stop and try jam?
there were more people standing still when there were 24 , about 60 percent than when there were six , about 40 percent .
and then we looked at in which case people rather bought a jar of jam .
now we saw the reverse effect .
of the people who stopped when it was 24 , only three percent actually bought a jar of jam .
of the people who stopped when they were six , well , now we saw that 30 percent of them actually bought a jar of jam .
well , if you figure it out , people would rather buy a jar six times if they had six to choose from than if they had 24 to choose from .
well , deciding not to buy jar jam is probably good for us - at least it &apos;s good for our waist - but it turns out that the congestion problem affects us even in very consequential decisions .
we decide not to decide , even if it is against our own best interests .
so on the topic of the day: financial savings .
i will now describe to you a study that i conducted with gur huberman , emir kem- jica , wei jang , in which we considered the decisions on savings plans for the pension of nearly one million americans from approximately 650 plans throughout the united states .
and what we were looking at was whether the number of fund offerings that were possible in a pension plan , the plan , the likelihood of whether more is saved for tomorrow .
and we found out that there was indeed a connection .
so in these plans , we had about 657 plans that offered to the people anything between two to 59 different funds .
and what we found out was that the more funds were offered that , in fact , the attendance rate was lower .
looking at the extreme now , the plans offered by the two funds had a participation rate in the seventies - still not as high as we would like .
in the plans , which offered nearly 60 funds , the participation rate now fell to about the sixtieth percent .
now it turns out that even if they decide to participate , if more choices are available , even then , it has negative consequences .
for example , people who choose to participate more in the sense that the more opportunities they have , the more they avoid choosing stocks and funds .
the more opportunities they had , the more they put their money into pure money market accounts .
none of these extreme decisions are among the decisions that someone would recommend to people if their future financial well-being is important to us .
well , over the past decade , we &apos;ve seen three major negative consequences for people , which means offering more and more choices .
they postpone the decision - postpone it , even if it is against their own best interests .
they tend to make worse decisions - worse financial and poorer medical decisions .
they tend to choose things that make them less satisfied , even if they make things better .
the main reason for this is that we might enjoy looking at these huge shelves full of mayonnaise , mustard , vinegar and jam , but in fact we can not draw the comparisons and demarcations and then actually choose something from the fantastic offerings .
so what i would like to suggest to you today are four simple techniques - techniques that we have tested in different ways and in different research locations - that you can easily apply to your business .
first , restrict .
they &apos;ve heard it before , but it &apos;s never been truer than today that less is more .
people are always worried when i say , &quot; limit . &quot;
they always worry about losing shelf space .
but in truth , we see it more and more and more that when they are ready to confine themselves , and the unimportant similar possibilities are released , well , then there is an increase in sales , the costs are lowered , & amp; amp; and there is an improvement in decision-making .
when proctor and gamble reduced the various head and shoulders types from 26 to 15 , they saw their sales increase by 10 percent .
when the golden cat corporation got rid of its 10 worst-selling feline strains , they saw profits increase 87 percent , a result of both the increase in sales and the reduction in costs .

what brings me to the second technique , which helps you to deal with the problem of decision overloading , it is the concretization .
the point is that in order to understand the difference between possibilities , people must understand which consequences are associated with the individual possibilities , and that these consequences must be felt vividly and concretely .
why , on average , do people spend 15 to 30 percent more on an ec card or credit card than cash?
because it does not feel like real money .
and it turns out that if you make it feel concrete , it &apos;s a great tool to make people save more .
so , in a study i made with shlomo benartzi and alessandro previtero , we did a study with the people at ing - employees who all worked for ing - and these people were all in a gathering in the you have registered your participation for your pension plan .
and during this gathering , we left this gathering exactly as it always was , only we added a little something .
the little thing we added was that we asked people to just think about all the positive things that would happen in their lives if they saved money .
by doing this simple thing , there was a 20% increase in attendance and there was an increase in the number of people who were willing to save , or a four percent increase in the amount they were willing to pay for the savings account ,
the third technique: categorization .
we can deal better with more categories than with more choices .
here &apos;s , for example , a study we did in a magazine review .
it turns out that in the wegmans supermarkets up and down the northeast area , the number varies from 331 different types of magazines to up to 664 magazines .
but do you know what?
if i show them 600 magazines and divide them into 10 categories or i show you 400 magazines and divide them into 20 categories , then i believe i gave them more choice and a better selection experience than i gave them the 400 if i gave them the 600
because the categories tell me how to tell them apart .
here are two different jewelry displays .
one is called &quot; jazz &quot; and the other is called &quot; swing &quot; ,
if you think that the left indicator is swing and the indicator on the right is jazz , then clap your hands .
okay , there are a few .
if you think the one on the left is jazz and the one on the right is swing then please clap .
okay , a few more .
well , it turns out they &apos;re right .
the one on the left is jazz and the one on the right is swing , but you know what?
this is a highly useless categorization scheme .
the categories must tell the selector something not to which the selection provides .
and you often see this problem when you look at the long lists of all these funds .
who do you want to inform?
my fourth technique: requirement for complexity .
it turns out that we can deal with more information than we think , we just have to simplify it .
we have to increase the complexity slowly .
i &apos;ll show you an example of what i mean .
let us take a very , very complicated decision: buying a car .
here is a german car manufacturer who offers you the possibility to configure your car completely yourself .
you have to make 62 different decisions to completely design your car .
now these choices differ in the number of choices they make per decision .
car color , exterior color of the car - i have 56 options .
motors , gearboxes - four options .
what i am doing now is changing the order in which the decisions appear .
the half of the customers goes from the many possibilities , 56 car colors to the low possibilities , four transmissions .
the other half of the customers goes from the small options , four gearboxes , to the 56 car colors , many possibilities .
what do i look at?
how interested are you .
if you constantly use the standard selection in the decision , that means that they are overwhelmed , that is , i lose them .
what you find out is that people who go from many to low selection options choose the standard selection over and over again .
we lose it .
go from the few choices to the many , then stick to it .
it &apos;s the same information . it is the same number of possibilities .
the only thing that i have done is change the order in which this information is presented .
when i start with the simple one , i learn how to pick .
even though picking the gear does not tell me about my interior design preferences , it still prepares me to pick .
it also excites me about the great product that i put together , so i &apos;m ready to be motivated to be there with interest .
let me summarize .
i told you about four techniques that mitigate the problem of decision overloading - limit - they get rid of redundant alternatives; concretize - make it real; categorize - we can deal better with more categories , less options; to prepare for complexity .
any of the techniques i am describing to you today is designed to help you manage the possibilities - good for you , you can use them for yourself , good for the people you serve .
because i believe that the key to the best possible choice is that one is picky in the selection .
and the more we are picky at picking , the better we will be able to practice the art of decision making .

how can i talk about the interconnectedness of women over three generations in ten minutes , and how the amazing strength of this bondage manifested itself in the life of a four-year-old girl when she was with her little sister , her mother and her grandmother over five years ago huddled in a small boat in the chinese sea for long nights . a bond that became firmly rooted in the life of this little girl and never disappeared - this little girl who now lives in san francisco and speaks to them today?
the story is not over yet .
she is a puzzle that is still put together .
i would like to tell you about some of the puzzle pieces .
imagine the first piece: a man who burns his life &apos;s work .
he is a poet , a writer , a man whose whole life was held together by the simple hope of unity and freedom of his homeland .
as the communists marched into saigon , remember how he had to admit that his life had been a waste .
the words , as long as his friends mocked his now .
he withdrew into the quiet .
he died , broken by the story .
he is my grandfather .
i never met him in person .
but our lives are so much more than our memories .
my grandmother never let me forget his life .
i had to make sure that it was not in vain , and it was my job to learn that the story was trying to crush us , but we survived it .
the next puzzle piece shows how a boat in the early morning red quietly runs out to sea .
my mother mai was 18 when her father died - already in an arranged marriage , already with two little girls .
for her , life had set her a task: the flight of her family and a new life in australia .
it was completely out of the question that she could fail .
after a four-year-old and cinematic saga , a boat glided silently onto the lake , camouflaged as a fishing boat .
all adults were aware of the risks .
the biggest fear was piracy , rape and death .
like most adults on the boat , my mother carried a small poison bottle with her .
when i was captured , my sister and i , and then she and my grandmother would have drunk .
my first memories are of this boat - the steady chugging of the engine , the clapping of each wave on the bow , the wide and empty horizon .
i do not remember the pirates who came many times , but were fooled by the death-despair of the men on our boat , or by the failure of the engine , which did not want to start six hours .
but i remember the lights of the oil platform off the malaysian coast , and the young man who collapsed and died , the end of the journey was too much for him , and the taste of the first apple that one of the men on the platform gave .
no apple has ever tasted like this again .
after three months in a refugee camp , we landed in melbourne .
and the next piece of the puzzle is about four women in three generations who are building a new life together .
we settled in footscray , a working-class suburb whose population consists of immigrant strata .
unlike the old-established mid-range suburbs whose existence was completely unknown to me , there was no pretension in footscray .
the smells from the shop doors came from the rest of the world .
and the scraps of broken english were exchanged between people who had one thing in common: they started afresh .
my mother worked on farms , then on a production line in a car factory , six days , two shifts .
somehow she managed to find the time to acquire english and it-qualifications .
we were poor .
every dollar was allocated and put into further education for english and math , no matter what we had to do without . mostly new things were always second-hand .
two pairs of stockings for school , each to cover the holes in the other .
a school uniform to the ankles , because she had to reach six years .
and there were the rare but painful choirs of &quot; schlitzauge &quot; and here and there murals: &quot; asians , go home . &quot;
home where to go?
something stiffened in me .
there was a meeting for reconciliation and a quiet voice said , &quot; i &apos;ll get you out of the way . &quot;
my mother , my sister and i slept in the same bed .
my mother was exhausted every night , but we told each other about our day and listened to the movements of our grandmother in the house .
my mother suffered from nightmares from the boat .
and it was my job to stay awake every night until her nightmares came so i could wake her up .
she opened a computer business , then did a baker &apos;s education and started another business .
and the women came with their stories about men who did not stand the change , angry and inflexible , and caring children trapped between two worlds .
loans and sponsors were sought .
centers were set up .
i lived in parallel worlds .
in one , i was the classic asian student who made inexorable demands on himself .
in the other , i was involved in insecure lives carrying tragic scars of violence , drug abuse and isolation .
but so many got help over the years .
and because of this help , in my final year of law school , i was selected as the young australian of the year .

tanle , an anonymous resident of footscray , was now a refugee and social activist invited to lectures she had never heard of and to dwellings she could never have imagined .

i am here to share my photography with you .

photos like this: a construction site along a busy street .

and still keep a realistic level .

when i say reality , i mean photo-reality .
because , of course , it is not something that you can really capture , but i always want to make it look as if you could have , in a photo .
photos where you have to think for a moment to find out the trick .

is it about the light?
what does the illusion create?
sometimes the perspective is the illusion .
but ultimately it &apos;s about how we interpret the world and how it can be made tangible on a two-dimensional surface .
it &apos;s not about what &apos;s realistic , but what we think is realistic .
so i think the basics are very simple .
i see it as a puzzle of reality , where you can take different pieces and put them together to create an alternative reality .
and let me show you a simple example .
here we have three perfectly imaginable physical objects that we can all identify with in a three-dimensional world .
but combined in a certain way , they can create something that still looks three-dimensional , as if it could exist .
but at the same time we know that that is not possible .
so we outsmart our brains , because our brain simply does not accept that it does not really make sense .
and i see the same process in combining photos .
it &apos;s really just about combining different realities .
so the things that make a picture look realistic , i think it &apos;s the things we do not think about , the things around us in our everyday lives .
but when we combine photos , it &apos;s really important to remember , because otherwise they just look wrong somehow .
i would say that there are three simple rules to follow to achieve a realistic result .
as you can see , these are not three special pictures .
combined but you can create something special like this .
the first rule is that combined photos should have the same perspective .
secondly , combined photos should have the same lighting style .
and these two pictures meet these two requirements - shooting from the same height and the same lighting .
thirdly , it is about making it impossible to distinguish where the individual images begin and end by connecting them seamlessly .
makes it impossible to say how the photo was actually put together .
by blending color , contrast and brightness along the edges with individual images , adding photographic flaws such as depth of field , desaturated colors and disturbances , we blur the boundaries between the different images and make them look like a single photo , even though there are basically hundreds of images can contain from levels .
here is another example .
one may think that this is just a landscape picture , and the lower part has been manipulated .
this picture is in reality completely composed , from photos of different places .
i personally think that . that it is easier to create a place than to find a place , because you do not have to compromise with the ideas in your head .
but it requires a lot of planning .
and when i got that idea in the winter , i knew that i would need several months to plan it , find the different places for the pieces of the puzzle .
for example , the fish was taken on a fishing trip .
the shore comes from a different place .
the underwater part comes from a quarry .
and yes , i even changed the house red on the top of the island to make it look more swedish .
in order to achieve a realistic result , it goes from planning to planning .
i always start with a sketch , an idea .
then it &apos;s about the combination of the different pictures .
and here each piece is very well planned .
and then , when you make good shots , the result can be pretty nice , and also quite realistic .
so all the tools are there , and the only thing that limits us is our imagination .

it shows how the government could work better - no longer as a private company , as many people think it should .

now there is a large community of people who make the tools we need to effectively tackle things together .
