normally , my role is to explain to people how wonderful the new technologies that are coming to be will be . and i thought , since i am here with friends , i would tell you what i really think . and also trying to look back and understand what is really going on , with those incredible leaps forward that are so swift that we can barely keep up .
i &apos;ll start by showing you a single , boring slide .
 . . . could you please briefly show the slide .
this is a foil , which i have chosen randomly from my folder .
i am not concerned with the details of the film , but rather with the general form .
this slide shows data from an analysis by us of the performance of risc processors compared to the performance of a local area network .
the interesting thing here is that this film , like some of the others that we often encounter in our field , is a kind of straight on a semi-logarithmic curve .
in other words: every step here corresponds to a potential in the performance scale .
and that is a novelty that we have to use this type scale on semi-logarithmic curves in the field of technology .
something very strange happens here .
that &apos;s essentially what i &apos;m going to talk about here .
so , if you could turn the light on again .
a little lighter , because i &apos;ll draw something on the paper here .
well , why do we draw graphs in technology on semi-logarithmic curves?
the reason for this is if i would draw it on a normal curve , where , say , this is the years , so the timeline of a kind , and this is any measure of technology that i want to plot with a graph , then the graph will be silly .
they look like this .
and that does not tell us much .
if i want to record another technology , let &apos;s say a transport technology , that would be very unreasonable on the semi-logarithmic scale , in the form of a flat line .
but if something like this happens , then it changes the qualitative circumstances .
so , if the transport technology progressed as fast as the microprocessor technology , then the day after tomorrow we could get into a taxi and in 30 seconds we would be in tokyo .
but that does not work so fast .
this has never happened before in the history of technology development , this kind of recursive growth that exponentially increases in size every few years .
the question i want to ask you is , if you look at these exponential curves , you see , they do not go on forever .
it is not possible that everything will evolve forever and ever as fast as now .
there are two possibilities :
it will either turn into a classic s curve like this , until something completely new comes along , or it will happen here .
that &apos;s about it .
well , i &apos;m optimistic , so i think something like this is going to happen here .
is that really how it is , then that means we are in the middle of a transition right now .
we are here on this line , in a transition from the previous world to a new kind of world .
so the question that i would like to ask , and i often ask myself , is how this new world will be?
what is this new state that the world is slowly taking on?
because the transition seems to be very , very confusing when you are in the middle of it .
when i was a kid , the future was somewhere in the year 2000 and people often talked about what would happen in 2000 .
now we have a conference where people talk about the future and you realize that the future is still in the year 2000 .
we can not go further than that .
in other words , the future has shrunk by a year each year since i was in this world .
i think the reason for that is that we realize that something is going on .
the transition happens . we can all feel it .
and we know that at the moment it just does not make sense to try to look 30 or 50 years into the future because everything will be so different that a simple extrapolation from what we do now just does not make sense .
i would like to talk about what that might be , this transition in which we are currently .
to do this i will first talk about some other things that really have nothing to do with technology or computers .
because i believe that in order to really understand this we need to step back and look at the matter on a longer time scale .
i want to look at this on the timescale of earth &apos;s life .
i think the overall picture makes sense , if you divide it into a few billion-steps .
so let &apos;s go back , 2.5 billion years . the earth was a large , sterile lump of rock with many chemicals floating on it .
now , looking at the way these chemicals were organized , this gives us a pretty good picture of how they did it .
i believe , too , there are theories that begin to understand how everything started with rna , but i will tell you a simplified story of it that at that time there were small oil droplets floating around , containing various types of chemical formulations .
some of these oil droplets contained a special combination of chemicals that allowed them to integrate other chemicals from the outside and thus grow .
everyone who was like that began to split up .
these small droplets of oil were in their sense the most primitive cell form .
however , these oil droplets were not alive in the present sense , because each of them was just a small , accidental recipe of chemicals .
and every time it split , the chemicals in it have become unevenly distributed .
so every droplet was a little different .
in fact , the droplets that had a composition that was more optimal for growth grew faster than others and integrated more and more chemicals from the outside and parted faster .
so they tended to survive longer and they multiplied to a greater extent .
so that &apos;s just a very simple chemical form of life , but it gets more interesting when these droplets learn a trick about abstraction .
in some way , which we still do not quite understand , these droplets have learned the ability to write down information .
they have learned to save the cell &apos;s recipe as information . in a certain chemical form , which we call dna .
in other words , in this mindless , evolutionary way , they have found a way that allowed them to write down what they were made of , so that this information could be stored and copied .
the amazing thing is that this kind of information storage remained the same for over 2.5 billion years in which it was created .
in fact , the recipe for us , our genes , consists of the exact same code and the same spelling .
every creature uses exactly the same letters and the same code .
we are ready to write something in this code for the sake of pleasure .
and here i have 100 micrograms of a white powder , which i try to keep away from security personnel in airports .
in it is - what i &apos;ve done , i &apos;ve taken this code - the code consists of normal letters , which we use to symbolize - and i have written my business card on a piece of dna and have strengthened it 10 ^ 22 times .
so if someone wants one hundred million copies of my business card , i have enough for everyone in this room and even for everyone in this world , and it &apos;s right here .
if i had been a selfish person in reality , i would have written you on a virus and released it into the room .
so what &apos;s the next step?
writing down the dna was an interesting step .
and that &apos;s what these cells did - that made them happy for another billion years .
but then another , very interesting step happened where the things became completely different . in fact , these cells began to communicate and share information , creating communities of cells .
i do not know if you already knew that , but bacteria can actually exchange their dna .
that is also the reason why , for example , the resistance to antibiotics grows .
some bacteria have figured out how to stay away from penicillin and have written this information along with other bacteria into the dna and now we have lots of bacteria that are resistant to penicillin because the bacteria communicate with each other .
this communication now allowed the founding of communities , which in some sense were in the same boat; they were synergetic .
thus they either survived together or they went under together . this meant that when a community was successful , all of the community &apos;s individuals were repeated more often and favored by evolution .
the transition point is now where these communities moved so close together that they decided to write the recipe for the whole community in a string on the dna .
and the next interesting phase lasted about another billion years .
at this time , we have multi-cellular communities , communities of many different types of cells , which together function as a single organism .
in fact , we are also such a multi-cellular community .
we have many cells that are no longer on their own .
your skin cells are useless without heart cells , muscle cells , brain cells and so on .
so these communities began to evolve , so that the plane on which the evolution took place was no longer the single cell , but the whole cellular community , which we call organism .
the next step was now within these communities .
the cell communities began to abstract information again .
and they developed special structures that did nothing other than process information within the organism .
and these are the neural structures .
the nerve cells are thus the information processing apparatus these cell communities have built up .
they started training specialists within the community , special structures that were responsible for learning , understanding and retaining information .
and this was the brain and the nervous system of these communities .
this gave them an evolutionary advantage .
because at this point in time , individual learning now occurred within the life span of a single organism rather than over that evolutionary time span .
for example , an organism could learn not to eat a particular fruit because it tasted bad and made it sick the last time he ate it .
this could be done within the lifetime of a single organism , whereas hundreds of thousands of years ago were needed , because this information had to be learned evolutionarily from the individuals who died because of this fruit , since these information processing structures were not yet available .
so the nervous system has the evolutionary process using these special information structures because evolution could now take place within an individual .
it could take place within a temporal learning framework .
what happened after that was that the individual organisms found a way to communicate with each other .
as an example , the most refined version of this type of communication known to us is the human language .
it &apos;s quite a startling invention if you think about it .
i have a very complicated , messy and confused idea in my head .
i sit here and basically make grunting notes of mine , hoping to create a similarly confused idea in your head that has a certain similarity to my original idea .
so we take something very complicated , transforming it into sounds , a sequence of sounds , producing something very complicated in the minds of others .
this allows us to function as a single organism again .
and what we have actually done , we , humanity , is , we have begun to abstract .
we go through the same stages that multicellular organisms have gone through - the abstraction of our methods , how we record , present , process data .
the invention of the language , for example , was a small step in this direction .
telephony , computers , video tapes , cd-roms and so on are all our specialized mechanisms that we have built in our community to handle this information .
and it connects us all together to something much bigger , and also able to evolve faster than we have been before .
nowadays evolution can happen in the period of microseconds .
you have also seen ty &apos;s little evolutionary example where he has a bit of evolution in the &quot; convolution &quot; program has shown right in front of your eyes .
so now we &apos;ve accelerated the timescale again .
the first steps in the story i told you took a billion years apiece .
the next steps , like the nervous system and the brain , lasted several hundred million years .
then the next steps , such as language and so on , took less than a million years .
and these next steps , like electronics , seem to take only a few decades .
the process feeds on itself and thus , i think , auto-catalytic is the word for it - if something increases its rate of change .
the more it changes , the faster it changes .
and i think that &apos;s what we see here in this explosive turn .
we see the process that feeds on itself .
i live professionally from building computers and i know that i could not use the mechanism i use to build computers without the recent advances in computer technology .
my current job is to design objects of such high complexity that it is impossible for me to design them in the traditional sense .
i do not know what each transistor does in the connection machine .
it has billions of those .
what i do instead , and what the designers of thinking machines do , is , we think on a certain level of abstraction , handing it over to the machine , and the machine makes it something that far surpasses our capabilities , better and faster than we ever do could .
and she does it partly with methods that we do not even understand right now .
a method that is particularly interesting , and which l have often used lately , is evolution itself .
we install an evolutionary process in the machine , which runs in the microscale range .
to give an example , in the most extreme cases , we can create a program that evolves from random instructions .
we say ,  &quot; computer , please generate one hundred million random sequences of instructions .
now please execute all these randomly chosen instructions , execute all the programs , and finally choose those that are closest to my specifications .  &quot;
so in other words , i first define what i want .
let &apos;s say i want to sort numbers , that would be a simple example i &apos;ve already used for that .
so find programs , which can sort the most likely numbers .
of course , the chance of randomly chosen instructions being very small is that they sort randomly by numbers , so probably none of the instructions will actually do that .
but one of them may , with luck , put two numbers in the right order .
now i say , &quot; computer , now take those 10% of the sequences that have come closest to my purposes .
save this , delete the rest .
now reproduce all the leftover ones that have sorted the numbers best .
namely with a method of recombining , analogous to reproduction .  &quot;
take two programs and you produce children through the exchange of their subroutines , and the children inherit the features of the subroutines from the two programs .
so now i have a new generation of programs created by combining programs that have come a bit closer to what they are .
say , &quot; please repeat this process . &quot;
she rates again .
maybe introduce some mutations .
and try the whole thing again with another generation .
all these generations take only a few milliseconds
and so i can go through millions of years of evolution in less than a few minutes . or in more complicated cases , some hours .
in the end i get programs that can sort numbers perfectly .
the fact is , these programs are so much more efficient than any program i could ever have written by hand .

so these are just a few of the clips i &apos;ve collected over the past six months - but it could also have been the last six days . or the last six years .
the fact is that the media prefer to show us negative issues because our minds pay close attention to these issues .
and there is a very good reason for that .
every second every day our senses capture much more information than our brain can ever handle .
and because nothing is more important to us than our survival , the first stop for all the information is part of our temporal lobe , the amygdala .
the amygdala is our early warning system , our danger detector .
it sorts and scours all the information in search of any indication of danger in our environment .
so when we see the news , we prefer to look for negative news .
and the old adage , &quot; if it bleeds , demand increases &quot; is very true .
now , with all of our digital devices delivering all of this negative news 24 hours a day , seven days a week , it &apos;s no wonder we &apos;re pessimistic .
it is no wonder that everyone thinks that the world is getting worse and worse .
but maybe this is not the case .
maybe that &apos;s the reality .
perhaps the tremendous progress that we have made in the last century through a series of forces will accelerate so much that in the next three decades we will be able to create a world of abundance .
well , i &apos;m not saying that we do not have big problems - climate crisis , species extinction , water and energy problems - we already have them .
and as humans , we are much better at anticipating problems in the very long term , but ultimately overcoming them .

in the 1980s , in communist east germany , if you had a typewriter , you had to register it with the government .
you also had to register a sheet with sample text from this typewriter .
the reason: the government could trace back where a text came from .
if you found an article with wrong mentality , you could track down the author of these thoughts .
and we in the west could not understand how anyone could do that , and how much that would limit the freedom of speech .
in our own countries , we would never do that .
but if you buy a new color laser printer from leading laser printer manufacturers in 2011 today and print a page , this page has bright yellow spots printed on each side , in a pattern that makes it easy to trace the page back to you and your printer .
that happens to us today .
and nobody seems to get upset about that .
and that was an example of how our own governments use technology against us , the citizens .
and that &apos;s one of the three main sources of today &apos;s online issues .
let &apos;s take a look at what &apos;s really happening in the world: we can split the attacks into categories .
we have three main groups .
there are online criminals .
for example , here is mr dimitry golubov from kiev in ukraine .
and the motives of online criminals are very easy to understand .
these people make money .
they use online attacks to make a lot of money , a huge pile of money .
there are several known cases of online millionaires , multimillionaires , who have earned their money through attacks .
this is wladimir tsastsin from tartu in estonia .
this is alfred gonzalez .
stephen watt .
björn sundin .
these are matthew anderson , tariq al-daour and so on and so forth .
these people have come to wealth online , but they have acquired it illegally by trojans have used it to steal money from our bank accounts while we do our online banking , or even keyloggers who have collected our credit card information when inputting via the keyboard while shopping online through an infected computer .
two months ago , the us secret service froze the swiss account of mr sam jain here , and on this account was $ 14.9 million when it was frozen .
mr . jain himself is on the loose , the place of residence is unknown .
and i claim that today we are more likely to become victims of online crime than a crime in the real world .
and it is very obvious that this will only get worse .
in the future , most crimes will play online .
the second largest group of attackers we see today is not motivated by money .
they are motivated by something else - protests about , opinion , or by their audience .
groups like anonymous have risen over the past 12 months and have become one of the main players in the field of online attacks .
so these are the three main assault groups: criminals who do it for money , hacktivists like anonymous , who resist , but the last group are nations , governments that lead the attacks .
there are cases like those of diginotar .
it exemplifies what happens when governments attack their own citizens .
diginotar is a certificate of attorney from the netherlands - or was that .
it had to file for bankruptcy last fall , because diginotar had been hacked .
someone had broken in and had hacked the system thoroughly .
and last week in a meeting with dutch government officials , i asked one of the leaders of this group if he thought it possible that people had died as a result of the diginotar hack .
and his answer was &quot; yes &quot; ,
well , how do people die as a result of such a hack?
diginotar is a certification body .
you sell certificates .
what do you do with certificates?
well , a certificate is needed if you have a website with https , with ssl-encrypted services , for example gmail .
now we use all , or many of us , gmail or one of your competitors . but these services are particularly prevalent in totalitarian states such as iran , where dissident foreign providers like gmail use , because they know that they can trust them more than local providers and that they are encrypted via ssl connections , so the local government does not can snoop around in their conversations .
but you can , if you are in a foreign
certification authority hacking and issuing fake certificates .
and that &apos;s exactly what happened in the case of diginotar .
how about the arab spring and the things that happened in egypt?
now , in april 2011 , insurgents raided the headquarters of the egyptian secret police . and they found a lot of files .
in these files was a folder called  &quot; finfisher &quot; ,
and this folder contained notes from a german-based company that had sold a number of programs to the egyptian government to intercept any communications from the egyptian citizens on a very large scale .
they had sold this program for 280,000 euros to the egyptian government .
the company headquarters is right here .
so western governments are providing totalitarian governments with tools to deal with their own citizens .
but western governments are also helping themselves .
for example , in germany just a few weeks ago the so-called state trojan horse found . this was a trojan used by german government officials to spy on their own citizens .
if you are suspected in a criminal case , then almost certainly your phone will be tapped .
but today we are far beyond that .
they tap into their internet connection .
they use things like the state trojan horse to populate their computer with a trojan that allows them to monitor all their communications , listen to their online discussions , collect their passwords .
so if we keep thinking about such things , then the obvious answer from the people would be: &quot; ok , that sounds bad , but it does not affect me , because i &apos;m a good citizen .
i do not have to worry!
i have nothing to hide .  &quot;
and this argument makes no sense .
privacy must be given .
privacy is not the debate .
it is not a decision between privacy and security .
it is a decision between freedom and control .
and while we may trust our governments today in 2011 , every right we give is given away forever .
and do we trust , blindly , to trust any future government , a government that we may have in fifty years?
these are the questions we have to deal with for the next 50 years .
it may seem strange to you , but i &apos;m a big fan of concrete blocks .
the first concrete blocks were made in 1868 and came from a simple idea: modular modules made of cement that fit together .
concrete blocks quickly became the most widely used building unit in the world .
they enabled us to build things larger than ourselves , buildings , bridges , one stone at a time .
essentially , the concrete element had become the building block of our time .
almost a hundred years later , in 1947 , lego came out with it .
it became the &quot; automatically connecting stone &quot; called  .
and within a few years , lego moved into every household .
it is estimated that over 400 billion stones were produced - or 75 stones for each person on this planet .
you do not have to be an engineer to create beautiful homes , beautiful bridges or beautiful buildings .
lego made it possible for everyone .
lego essentially took the concrete building block , the building block of the world , and turned it into a building block for our imagination .
in the meantime , the next revolution was announced in the same year at bell labs , the next building block .
the transistor was a small plastic unit that would take us from a world of static , stacked building blocks to a world where everything was interactive .
like the concrete block , the transistor allows us to build much larger , more complex cycles , one stone at a time .
but there is one main difference: the transistor was only for experts .
i personally do not accept that the building block of our time is reserved for experts and therefore i decided to change that .
eight years ago i was at the media lab and i started to explore this idea of ​​how to put the power of engineers in the hands of artists and designers .
a few years ago i started to develop littlebits .
let me show you how they work .
littlebits are electronic modules that have a specific function .
they are prefabricated to be light , sound , motor and sensor .
and the best part is that they connect with magnets together .
so you can not put them the wrong way around .
the stones are color coded .
green is output , blue is current , pink is input and orange is cable .
so all you have to do is connect one blue and one green and you can quickly build bigger circuits .
they add a blue to a green , and they can light up .
you can insert a switch in between and you have created a small dimmer .
take the switch out to insert a pulse module that is here , and you have made a small blinker .
add this buzzer for an extra effect and you have made a noise machine .
i will stop that .
beyond the simple play , littlebits is actually quite powerful .
instead of having to program , wire or solder , littlebits allows you to program them with simple , intuitive gestures .
so to speed up or slow down the flashing , simply turn this knob and it will make the pulse faster or slower .
the idea behind littlebits is that it &apos;s a growing library .
we want to bring every single interaction in this world into a ready-made stone .
lights , sounds , solar panels , motors - everything should be available .
we distributed littlebits to children and watched them play .
and it was an incredible experience .
the best part is how you begin to understand the electronics that surround you in everyday life and that you do not learn at school .
how , for example , a nightlight works , or why the doors of the elevator remain open , or how an ipod reacts to contact .
we also brought littlebits to design schools .
so , for example , we had designers with no experience in electronic things who started playing with littlebits as a material .
here we see , with felt , paper and water bottles , how geordie  . . .
a few weeks ago we took littlebits to risk and gave them to some designers who had no technical experience - just cardboard , wood and paper - and told them: &quot; do something . &quot;
here is an example of a project you made , a motion-controlled confetti cannon .
but wait , this is really my favorite project .
it is a lobster made of dough , which is afraid in the dark .
for these non-engineers , littlebits became another material , electronics just became another material .
and we want to make this material available to all .
so littlebits is open source .
you can go to the website , download all design templates and make them yourself .
we want to encourage the world of the creator , the inventor , the contributor , because the world in which we live , this interactive world , is ours .
so , move on and start inventing .
many thanks  .
